This thesis has been prepared over five months at the Section for Department of Applied
Mathematics and Computer Science, at the Technical University of Denmark, DTU, in
partial fulfilment for the degree Master of Science in Engineering, MSc Eng.
The thesis was developed between 20 January 2025 and 16 June 2025, with an assigned
workload of 30 ECTS credits.
The thesis was conducted under the supervision of Nicola Dragoni, Professor at DTU
Compute, and Maria Papaioannou, Postdoc at DTU Compute. It is assumed that the
reader has a basic knowledge in the areas of cybersecurity.

ii
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
Metehan Gelgi
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
Secure transmission of classified or sensitive information across multiple sites is a critical
requirement for government, military, and critical infrastructure sectors. Ensuring confi-
dentiality and integrity over untrusted networks like the public internet is a major challenge.
To address this, the NSA’s Commercial Solutions for Classified (CSfC) program devel-
oped the Multi-Site Connectivity (MSC) Capability Package. To address this, the NSA’s
Commercial Solutions for Classified (CSfC) program developed the Multi-Site Connectiv-
ity (MSC) Capability Package, which defines a dual-layer encryption architecture using
NIAP-approved commercial off-the-shelf (COTS) products.
This thesis investigates the feasibility of building secure, CSfC-compliant multi-site con-
nectivity architectures using both commercial and open-source solutions. Through hands-
on implementation and evaluation, we compare these approaches based on security, per-
formance, cost, and compliance with MSC CP requirements. We also explore European-
based alternatives to CSfC-approved products to identify practical options for organiza-
tions outside the U.S. These alternatives aim to provide comparable levels of security,
compliance, and interoperability without relying on CSfC components.
Our results demonstrate that both commercial and open-source solutions can satisfy the
core requirements of the CSfC MSC CP, including support for dual-layer IPsec encryption,
centralized management, and real-time monitoring. Commercial products offer certified
security and vendor support but often come with high costs. On the other hand, open-
source tools are more flexible and cost-effective, though they may require more time and
expertise to integrate. Overall, this thesis provides practical insights and recommenda-
tions for organizations planning to build secure multi-site communication systems, with a
focus on achieving strong security, reliable performance, and cost-efficiency.
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution

viii
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:11==
1
Introduction
In today’s interconnected world, many organizations operate across multiple locations.
This distributed structure requires strong and secure communication between sites. Trans-
mitting classified or sensitive information over untrusted networks introduces several se-
curity risks, including interception, eavesdropping, tampering and unauthorized access
[1]. To address these threats, National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) [2],
National Security Agency (NSA) [3] and other organizations have defined best practices
and frameworks for protecting data in transit. These guidelines and standards are de-
signed to protect the confidentiality, integrity, and availability of data.
In this context, various secure multi-site connectivity frameworks and solutions have been
developed. Site-to-Site Virtual Private Networks (VPNs) are a common approach, es-
tablishing encrypted tunnels over public infrastructure [4]. Software-Defined Wide Area
Networks (SD-WAN) offer more advanced capabilities, such as dynamic path selection
and centralized management, to enhance network performance and security [5]. Further-
more, commercial offerings often integrate cloud-based technologies like Virtual Private
Clouds (VPCs) for scalable connections [6] and emerging architectures like Palo Alto’s
Zero Trust Network Architecture (ZTNA) 2.0 represent a shift towards granular access
control and continuous verification [7].
A key initiative in this domain is the NSA’s Commercial Solutions for Classified (CSfC) pro-
gram. This program provides structured capability packages and requirements for protect-
ing classified data [8]. CSfC enables the use of commercial off-the-shelf (COTS) products
that meet strict security standards, providing a cost-effective alternative to Government-
of-the-Shelf (GOTS) solutions[9]. A key component of the CSfC program is the Multi-Site
Connectivity (MSC) Capability Package. This package defines a dual-layer encryption ar-
chitecture that securely connects geographically separated networks by transmitting data
through two independent, nested encryption tunnels over untrusted networks. The MSC
Capability Package is continuously reviewed and updated by the NSA to adapt to evolving
threats and security standards [10].
This research project was carried out in collaboration between the Danish Defence In-
telligence Service (DDIS) and the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). It addresses
DDIS’s requirement to securely transfer classified data to new locations. The primary ob-
jective is to evaluate whether the CSfC MSC Capability Package can meet DDIS’s security
and operational needs under various configurations.
1.1
Thesis Structure
This thesis is organized into 12 chapters, each contributing to the analysis, design, and
implementation of secure multi-site connectivity solutions based on the CSfC Multi-Site
Connectivity (MSC) Capability Package.
• Chapter 1 - Introduction: This chapter provides an overview of the research topic,
background, and objectives.
• Chapter 2 - Problem: This chapter defines the research questions, system require-
ments, scope, limitations, and summarizes the contributions of the thesis.
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
1
==PAGE:12==
Figure 1.1: Overview of the Thesis Structure
• Chapter 3 - CSfC Multi-Site Connectivity Capability Package and Relevant An-
nexes: This chapter describes the CSfC MSC Capability Package, high-level de-
sign, security principles, solution components, and relevant supporting annexes.
• Chapter 4 - Literature Review: This chapter examines existing literature on both
commercial CSfC-approved tools and open-source alternatives, with a focus on their
capabilities, security features, and their compliance with the MSC design.
• Chapter 5 - Methodology: This chapter outlines the research methodology applied
to analysing and comparing MSC Solutions. It defines the metrics used to evaluate
performance, security, and operational effectiveness in both implementations.
• Chapter 6 - System Architecture: This chapter presents the network design and
virtual environment setup for the proposed solutions, explaining key design deci-
sions based on the MSC Capability Package requirements.
• Chapter 7 - Commercial Solutions: This chapter details the implementation of
commercial tools following the CSfC MSC guidelines, including performance evalu-
ation, security validation, and cost analysis.
• Chapter 8 - Open-Source Solutions: This chapter identifies and implements open-
source tools that align with CSfC MSC requirements and evaluates their perfor-
mance, security compliance, and cost-effectiveness.
• Chapter 9 - Analysis and Comparison of Commercial and Open Source Solu-
tions: This chapter compares the commercial and open-source implementations,
focusing on security, performance, cost, and compliance with CSfC MSC require-
ments.
• Chapter 10 - Continuous Monitoring and Anomaly Detection of Open-Source
Solution: This chapter extends the open-source implementation by integrating cen-
tralized management and monitoring solutions. It implements security tests and
demonstrates anomaly detection capabilities.
• Chapter 11 - Discussion: This chapter interprets key findings, evaluates CSfC
compliance, addresses limitations, and revisits the research questions.
• Chapter 12 - Conclusion: The final chapter summarizes the research outcomes,
highlights key contributions, and suggests directions for future work.
2
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:13==
2
Problem
The secure transmission of classified data across multiple sites is a concern for the Dan-
ish Defence Intelligence Service (DDIS). For this problem, DDIS selected the Multi-Site
Connectivity (MSC) Capability Package to securely transfer classified data to new sites
over untrusted networks. This Capability Package mandates the use of CSfC-approved
commercial off-the-shelf (COTS) due to their compliance with security standards, NIAP
certification, and proven reliability. However, these commercial solutions present signif-
icant limitations. They frequently involve high licensing fees and recurring maintenance
costs.
In light of these challenges, DDIS seeks to explore open-source alternatives as a more
adaptable and cost-effective approach. Open-source tools require less expensive hard-
ware and eliminate proprietary licensing fees. The flexibility of open-source tools allows
for customization and seamless integration into diverse network architectures. Moreover,
the community-driven development of open-source software enables rapid innovation,
continuous updates, and collaborative responses to emerging security threats.
Despite these advantages, there are still open questions about whether open-source solu-
tions can meet the strict security, compliance, and performance requirements of classified
environments. DDIS needs to determine whether these tools can match the robustness
of CSfC MSC-compliant commercial solutions, scale to real-world deployments, and de-
liver comparable reliability. This uncertainty highlights a critical research gap for DDIS in
evaluating the viability of open-source security tools as replacements or supplements to
commercial CSfC implementations.
This thesis responds directly to DDIS’s request by investigating this gap. We conduct a
systematic analysis of the CSfC MSC framework and evaluate the feasibility of building
multi-site connectivity solutions using both commercial COTS products and open-source
tools. We compare the two approaches in terms of security posture, performance, scala-
bility, and cost-effectiveness. Through this work, we aim to provide actionable recommen-
dations for DDIS and similar organizations seeking secure, scalable, and cost-effective
multi-site connectivity solutions. Our findings contribute to both academic research and
real-world cybersecurity practices. The results help to bridge the gap between formal
policy requirements and the operational needs of modern solutions.
2.1
Research Questions
The primary question of this master thesis:
• RQ1: How can a secure multi-site connectivity solution be designed and imple-
mented to meet CSfC MSC requirements using both commercial and open source
tools?
To answer this research question, the thesis follows a systematic process. It begins with
understanding the security and compliance requirements, evaluating available tools, im-
plementing a solution, and assessing its effectiveness.
Other than the main research question, the following research questions guide this study:
• RQ2: What are the core requirements, security principles, and architectural com-
ponents of the CSfC MSC Capability Package?
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
3
==PAGE:14==
• RQ3: What are the key advantages, implementation challenges, and trade-offs of
designing and deploying a secure multi-site connectivity solution based on the CSfC
MSC Capability Package?
• RQ4: What are the characteristics, advantages, and limitations of commercial so-
lutions for secure multi-site connectivity?
• RQ5: What are the existing open-source solutions for secure multi-site connectivity,
and how do open-source solutions perform in terms of performance and security?
• RQ6: How do open-source solutions compare to commercial products for achiev-
ing CSfC MSC-compliant secure multi-site connectivity, in terms of security, perfor-
mance, cost, and needs of DDIS?
2.2
Requirements
To define the scope and considerations of this thesis, we conducted discussions with
DDIS. Based on these discussions, we identified key requirements that the solution must
fulfill. These requirements guide the study in terms of design, implementation, and eval-
uation of a secure multi-site connectivity solution.
• Multi-site connectivity solution must comply with the CSfC Multi-Site Connectivity
(MSC) Capability Package. In Chapter 3, we list the specific CSfC requirements
used in this thesis.
• All data exchanged between sites must be encrypted using NSA-approved algo-
rithms to ensure secure data transmission, confidentiality and integrity.
• The network architecture should be scalable, allowing the integration of additional
sites with minimal reconfiguration effort.
• The solution must support centralized management and enable real-time monitoring
of network traffic and system events.
• The network must maintain reasonable and realistic performance levels to satisfy
DDIS’s operational needs while adhering to the security standards and configura-
tions defined by the CSfC MSC CP.
• The solution must establish a clear network baseline that defines normal behavior,
such as typical traffic volumes, timing patterns, common protocols, and bandwidth
usage. Anomaly detection tools must monitor live traffic against anomalies.
• The open-source solution must use only open-source tools. This means there should
be no need to pay for access to full features or advanced functionality.
2.3
Scope and Limitations
This section defines the scope and limitations of implementing secure multi-site connec-
tivity solutions based on the NSA’s CSfC MSC Capability Package, evaluating both open-
source and commercial approaches in controlled virtual environments.
2.3.1
Scope
• CSfC MSC Capability Package: We review and explain the CSfC Multi-Site Con-
nectivity (MSC) Capability Package and its key requirements.
• Design and Implementation: We design and implement secure multi-site network
topologies using both open-source and commercial tools. Cisco Modeling Labs is
4
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:15==
used to simulate the commercial solution, while VMware ESXi is used to run open-
source security tools in virtual machines.
• Security Assessment: We evaluate the security of both setups by looking at en-
cryption standards, authentication methods, and how well they meet CSfC MSC
requirements.
• Performance Evaluation: We measure and compare performance in terms of
throughput, latency, and resource usage for both solutions.
• Cost Analysis: We analyze and compare the hardware and software costs of com-
mercial and open-source solutions. Total Cost of Ownership (Licensing, Deploy-
ment, Support, and Maintenance costs) is out of scope of this thesis. On the con-
trary, the focus of this thesis is on the analysis of market prices for the deployed
components.
• Comparative Analysis: We have a comprehensive comparison of open-source
and commercial tools, including strengths and weaknesses of the two approaches.
We discuss how they perform in a CSfC-compliant setup.
• Network Monitoring and Anomaly Detection: We set up continuous network
monitoring based on the Continuous Monitoring Annex and apply anomaly detection
methods to identify unusual traffic patterns.
• Focus on IPsec Implementation: While the CSfC MSC Capability Package allows
for encryption through both IPsec and Media Access Control Security (MACsec), this
work does not address MACsec related requirements or implementations. MACsec
is not included due to its different requirements and added complexity, which are
beyond the scope of this master thesis research project.
• Full Scope of Annexes: We address several annexes of the MSC Capability Pack-
age, but a full implementation of all annex requirements is outside the scope of this
thesis.
2.3.2
Limitations
• Virtual Environment Limitations: We carried out the implementation and testing
in virtual environments (VMware ESXi and Cisco Modeling Labs). These setups are
useful for simulation but do not fully reflect real-world conditions. Performance and
system behaviour may differ on physical hardware.
• Resource Constraints: The tests depend on the available virtual resources and
hardware, which influence the performance results observed.
• Tool Selection: This study focuses on a limited set of both open-source and com-
mercial tools. Due to high costs, it was not feasible to include all available solutions
or to test commercial tools outside a virtual environment. As a result, the findings
may not be fully generalizable to all solutions for secure multi-site connectivity. The
tools were selected based on the specific requirements of the thesis and their com-
mon use in industry practice.
• Compliance Scope: While we followed the CSfC MSC guidelines, we did not in-
clude all aspects of compliance and requirements such as the use of NIAP-certified
hardware components, physical security requirements, or formal CSfC certification
steps.
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
5
==PAGE:16==
• Cost Estimation: The cost analysis is based on estimated costs and may not re-
flect the actual costs incurred by organizations implementing these solutions. We
do not cover licensing or support costs, as these usually require specific business
agreements.
• Time Constraints: Due to time limitations, we prioritized key goals and require-
ments of the project. As a result, we did not address some aspects of the solution,
such as ensuring high availability and conducting extensive performance testing.
By defining this scope and limitations, we aim to provide a focused analysis of secure
multi-site connectivity solutions within realistic and practical boundaries.
2.4
Thesis Contributions
In this thesis, we contribute to the field of secure network communications, specifically
within the context of the NSA’s Commercial Solutions for Classified (CSfC) Multi-Site Con-
nectivity (MSC) framework. Our main contributions are:
1. Comprehensive Analysis of CSfC MSC Requirements: We explain and analyze
the CSfC MSC Capability Package and its related annexes to build a comprehensive
understanding of the package.
2. Design and Implementation of Network Architecture for CSfC MSC: We design
and implement a secure multi-site connectivity solution that follows both the CSfC
MSC guidelines and the needs of DDIS. We provide practical guidance for building
such a system using both open-source and commercial tools.
3. Comparative Analysis of Open-Source and Commercial Solutions: We provide
a comprehensive comparison of open-source and commercial tools for implement-
ing CSfC-compliant multi-site connectivity. We evaluate their respective strengths
and weaknesses in terms of security, performance, and cost-effectiveness. We also
check how well they meet CSfC MSC requirements.
4. Evaluation of Open-Source Viability for CSfC: We investigate the feasibility of
using open-source tools to meet the stringent security and compliance requirements
of the CSfC framework. The findings help organizations assess the potential of
open-source solutions as a cost-effective alternative to commercial solutions.
5. Cost Analysis: We conduct a detailed cost analysis of hardware and software com-
ponents for both types of solutions. The findings help organizations assess the po-
tential of open-source solutions as a cost-effective alternative to commercial offer-
ings. They can make informed decisions based on their specific budget constraints
and security requirements.
With these contributions, we aim to support DDIS and other organizations in navigating the
complexities of CSfC-compliant secure multi-site connectivity. Our findings offer insights
into security, performance, cost, and regulatory needs of MSC CP solutions in real-world
implementations.

Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:17==
3
CSfC Multi-Site Connectivity Capability
Package and Relevant Annexes
In this chapter, we provide a comprehensive overview of the CSfC MSC Capability Pack-
age. We explain its architecture, encryption requirements, component selection, and se-
curity considerations. Although the MSC Capability Package is the main focus of our
research, it is supported by several annexes. We discuss these annexes with the MSC
CP to ensure a complete understanding of the requirements.
• Multi-Site Connectivity Capability Package [10]: Describes how to protect clas-
sified data over untrusted networks using dual encryption methods such as IPsec
and MACsec. It is designed to be adaptable for a wide range of applications.
• Enterprise Gray Implementation Requirements Annex [11]: Guides to expand
Multi-Site networks across large geographic distances. It allows the combination
of multiple CSfC Capability Packages[8], supports centralized management, and
improves scalability.
• Key Management Requirements Annex [12]: Guides the use of asymmetric en-
cryption for CSfC Data-in-Transit (DIT) solutions. It includes best practices for using
Public Key Infrastructure (PKI), strong password policies, and digital certificates.
• Symmetric Key Management Requirements Annex [13]: Specifies the use of
symmetric pre-shared keys (PSKs) to offer quantum-resistant protection for classi-
fied data. It also defines roles and requirements for managing symmetric keys.
• Continuous Monitoring Annex [14]: Outlines the requirements for implementing
continuous monitoring (CM) within CSfC solutions. It focuses on collecting and an-
alyzing network and security data in real time.
3.1
Description and High Level Design of Multi-Site
Connectivity CP
Multi-Site Connectivity (MSC) Capability Package is designed to establish secure, clas-
sified communication across multiple locations. This package was previously named as
VPN Capability Package (VPN CP). The latest MSC CP, version 1.2.0, was released on
March 2, 2023. This CP outlines a general MSC solution to protect classified information
as it traverses untrusted networks or networks operating at different security levels. The
solution supports interconnecting two or more networks operating at the same security
level via encryption tunnels.
The MSC CP provides high-level reference designs and corresponding configuration re-
quirements. This allows customers to select COTS products from the CSfC Components
List for their MSC solution and properly configure these products to achieve a level of as-
surance sufficient to protect classified data-in-transit. Open-source components can also
be used if they have a responsible sponsor and an NSA-approved plan for evaluation and
long-term support [15].
MSC CP and annexes provide a list of requirements to implement secure multi-site con-
nectivity solutions. These requirements are numbered with capital letters corresponding
part of the solution. For example, ”MSC-VG-1” is the first requirement of VPN Gateways
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
7
==PAGE:18==
in Multi-Site Connectivity CP and ”CM-LN-1” represents the first Logging Requirement in
the Continuous Monitoring annex. When we describe parts of the CP or its components,
we include the related requirement numbers to point back to the official documentation.
3.2
High Level Design
The CSfC Multi-Site Connectivity (MSC) Capability Package uses a layered encryption
approach with two separate, nested encryption tunnels to ensure the confidentiality and
integrity of data in transit. These tunnels can be implemented using Internet Protocol
Security (IPsec) generated by a Virtual Private Network (VPN) Gateway or Media Access
Control Security (MACsec) generated by a MACsec Device. VPN Gateways and MACsec
Devices are implemented as part of the network infrastructure. This dual-layered encryp-
tion approach provides robust protection against potential threats on untrusted networks.
High-level MSC Solution Architecture is given in Figure 3.1.
Figure 3.1: MSC Network Design (based on [10])
The MSC solution architecture is built around three network layers:
• Red Network: This network contains unencrypted classified data and is located
behind the Inner Encryption Component
• Gray Network: This layer carries classified data that has been encrypted once.
• Black Network: This is an untrusted network (such as the public internet) where
data travels in its twice-encrypted form.
When data needs to be transmitted from the Red Network to a remote site, it undergoes
two layers of encryption before traversing the Gray and Black Networks. Upon reaching
the destination, the data is decrypted in the reverse order to restore it to its original form.
Each layer of the MSC solution can utilize either an IPsec tunnel or a MACsec tunnel.
An IPsec tunnel is established between VPN Gateways, while a MACsec tunnel is es-
tablished between MACsec Devices. However, as outlined in the scope (Section 2.3.1),
we focus only on IPsec-based implementations using VPN Gateways. MACsec-related
configurations and requirements are not included in the scope of this work.
3.2.1
Management Configurations
The MSC Capability Package supports different management configurations based on
organizational needs.
Independently Managed Sites
In this configuration, each site independently manages
its encryption components. Management traffic does not traverse the Black Network. This
ensures full operational independence between sites. It is a good choice for organizations
8
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:19==
that need autonomy while still maintaining secure data sharing. This architecture is shown
in Figure 3.2.
Figure 3.2: Independently Managed MSC Architecture
Centrally Managed Sites
Alternatively, the MSC solution can be set up with central-
ized management. In this model, one central site manages the encryption components
across all remote locations. As shown in Figure 3.3, management traffic is encrypted
at least twice before passing through the Black Network, ensuring its confidentiality and
integrity. In this setup, it is important to separate data tunnels from management tun-
nels. Central management must adhere to the guidelines outlined in the CSfC Enterprise
Gray Implementation Requirements Annex[11]. Detailed explanation of central manage-
ment requirements and configurations is explained under Gray Enterprise Annex (Section
3.6.2).
Figure 3.3: Centrally Managed MSC Architecture
3.3
Security and Design Features of MSC CP
The MSC solution architecture provides key security principles and design attributes that
ensure robust protection for data-in-transit.
• Confidentiality: The MSC solution ensures that classified information remains con-
fidential during transmission across untrusted networks. This is achieved through
the use of two nested, independent encryption tunnels, typically implemented us-
ing Internet Protocol Security (IPsec) protocols via Virtual Private Network (VPN)
Gateways.
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
9
==PAGE:20==
• Integrity: By utilizing NSA-approved cryptographic algorithms from the Commercial
National Security Algorithm (CNSA) Suite, the MSC solution ensures that data is not
tampered with during transit. This guarantees the authenticity and reliability of the
transmitted information.
• Scalability: The MSC Capability Package is designed to support the interconnec-
tion of multiple sites operating at the same security level. This scalability allows
organizations to expand their networks without significant reconfiguration, facilitat-
ing the addition of new sites as operational needs evolve.
• Adaptability: The MSC Capability Package offers flexibility in its implementation,
recognizing that different organizations have different needs. It offers a variety of
configuration options through its main package and supporting annexes. This al-
lows organizations to adjust the solution to fit their specific operations while still
meeting the required security standards for protecting classified information. The
CSfC program allows the use of open source components, as long as there is an
NSA-approved plan to evaluate and maintain them through the Common Criteria
process. This adaptability ensures that organizations can tailor the MSC solution
to their specific operational needs while maintaining compliance with security stan-
dards.
• Auditability: This MSC Capability Package requires comprehensive logging and
monitoring mechanisms to support auditability. This includes the deployment of Se-
curity Information and Event Management (SIEM) systems on both the Red and
Gray networks. This Capability Package also requires daily reviews of these logs
by auditors to detect and respond to any anomalies or unauthorized activities.
• Availability and Redundancy: The primary focus of the MSC CP is on confidential-
ity and integrity. While high availability is not the primary focus of the MSC CP, the
architecture permits the incorporation of redundant components to enhance system
resilience. Organizations can add backup Inner and Outer Encryption Components
and build redundant network paths to ensure continuous operation during failures.
These setups can also support load balancing to increase overall performance.
Figure 3.4: MSC Solution Design with Availability
Figure 3.4 shows an example where each site includes two Outer Encryption Com-
ponents. These are connected to the Black Network through separate tunnels. A
router within the Gray Network selects the appropriate tunnel for outbound traffic.
This design ensures that the site remains connected even if one component fails.
10
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:21==
3.4
Networks
MSC CP defines 3 types of networks that refer to the different levels of protection applied
to the corresponding network.
• Red Network: This network is considered the most secure network, where clas-
sified data exists in unencrypted form. It is located behind the Inner Encryption
Component (IEC) at both the central and remote sites. A dedicated Red Manage-
ment Workstation (Red MW) manages this network through the Red Management
Network (MSC-DM-4). There can be one or more distinct Red Networks behind the
IEC. If these Red Networks operate at different security levels, they must be sep-
arated by a Gray Firewall (see Section 3.5.3) and are not allowed to communicate
with each other (MSC-SR-7).
• Gray Network: After data is encrypted once by the Inner Encryption Component,
it enters the Gray Network. This network carries single-encrypted classified data
and includes components such as the Outer Encryption Component, Gray Firewall,
and Gray Management Services. Similar to the Red Network, the Gray Network
is managed by a dedicated Gray Management Workstation (Gray MW) over the
Gray Management Network (MSC-DM-5). The Gray Data and Gray Management
networks must be at minimum logically separated using access control lists (ACLs)
on the Gray Firewall (MSC-DM-25).
• Black Network: Before reaching the Black Network, the single-encrypted data is
encrypted again by the Outer Encryption Component, creating a second encryption
layer. The Black Network represents an untrusted environment, such as the public
internet. In this layer, data is protected by dual-layer encryption. Unlike the Red and
Gray Networks, the Black Network may not be managed by a trusted management
workstation and is considered untrusted. If the Black Network is the public internet,
an Outer Firewall is required between the Black Network and the Outer Encryption
Component (MSC-SR-12).
Network Traffics
The secure and efficient operation of the MSC solution depends on how network traffic is
managed and what protocols are used. This section explains the different types of traffic
handled by the solution and the protocols.
The MSC Solution defines three types of traffic planes:
• Data Plane Traffic: Data plane traffic consists of classified information that transits
through the network. The data can be either in unencrypted form (on the Red Net-
work) or encrypted form (on the Gray and Black Networks). The core function of the
MSC Solution is to encrypt and decrypt data plane traffic to ensure its confidentiality
and integrity.
In the Gray and Black Networks, data plane traffic is encapsulated using IPsec’s En-
capsulating Security Payload (ESP) or MACsec. These methods use strong encryp-
tion algorithms approved under the Commercial National Security Algorithm (CNSA)
Suite. The MSC solution also requires mutual authentication between devices:
– MACsec Devices authenticate using Pre-Shared Key (PSKs) called a Connec-
tivity Association Key (CAK), or they use EAP-TLS with 802.1X to exchange
public key certificates between devices.
– VPN Gateways authenticate using public key certificates and support RFC
8784-compliant IKEv2. They can also use a combination of PSK and public key
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
11
==PAGE:22==
certificates to ensure quantum-resistant security. Table 3.1 lists the approved
algorithms used for IPsec encryption as defined in requirement MSC-VG-1.
Table 3.1: IPsec Encryption Approved Algorithms

(Encryp-

The MSC solution supports both IPv4 and IPv6, making it adaptable across different
network environments. It is also flexible regarding higher-layer data protocols as
long as the chosen protocols meet the system’s security and performance goals.
• Management Plane Traffic: This traffic is responsible for configuring, monitoring,
and managing the system components. It includes communication between ad-
ministrators and devices. It also covers logs and status updates sent to a Security
Information and Event Management (SIEM) system or similar tools. In both the Red
and Gray Networks, management traffic is protected using secure protocols such
as SSH version 2 (SSHv2), IPsec, MACsec, or TLS 1.2 or later protocols. A fun-
damental security principle within the MSC solution is the requirement for physical
and cryptographic separation between data plane and management plane traffic.
Management plane traffic must only be initiated from a Management Workstation or
may be initiated from Outer Encryption Components for logging and authentication
traffic(MCS-PF-8).
• Control Plane Traffic: Control plane traffic consists of essential network proto-
cols that facilitate fundamental network operations. Unlike data or management
traffic, it is not initiated by users or administrators directly. Control Plane traffic in-
cludes Network address configuration(DHCP, NDP), Address resolution(ARP, NDP),
Time synchronization, Routing protocols(OSPF, BGP) and Certificate status distri-
bution(HTTP, CRLs). Network services provided by control plane protocols (such as
NTP) must be located on the inside network (i.e., Gray Network for Outer Encryption
Component and Red Network for Inner Encryption Component)(MSC-SR-1).
The MSC solution also supports standard routing technologies, such as GRE, MPLS,
or OSPF. These can be used within the Black and/or Red Networks, as long as they
are approved by the organization’s Authorizing Official (AO).
3.5
Solution Components and Requirements
In this section, we describe each component of the CSfC Multi-Site Connectivity (MSC)
solution by outlining its role and related requirements. To maintain focus, we excluded
requirements that are not relevant to the scope of this thesis. The full list of requirements
can be found in the MSC Capability Package [10]. While many requirements are specific
to individual components, some apply to all components across the solution. The general
requirements relevant to this thesis are summarized in Table 3.2.

of the component per the organization’s AO-approved Product Supply Chain Threat Assess-
ment process.
MSC-PS-16
MSC Solution Components must be configured to use the NIAP-certified evaluated configura-
tion.
3.5.1
Outer Firewall
When the public internet is used as the Black Network, the MSC solution requires an
Outer Firewall (MSC-SR-12). This firewall is located at the edge of the Gray Network and
acts as the first line of defense against external threats from the untrusted network.
This firewall must be physically and logically separated from the Outer Encryption Com-
ponent (OEC) (MSC-PS-12). Its external interface should only allow IPsec or MACsec
traffic that is specifically addressed to the remote OEC. All other types of traffic must be
blocked. On the internal side, the firewall should only forward approved, encrypted traffic
into the Gray Network. This ensures a strict separation between the untrusted Black Net-
work and the Gray Network. Table 3.3 provides a summary of the key configuration and
operational requirements for the Outer Firewall.
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
13
==PAGE:24==

CSfC Req #
Description
MSC-PS-3
The products used for any Firewalls must be chosen from the list of Traffic Filtering Firewalls
on the CSfC Components List.
MSC-PS-12
If an Outer Firewall and/or Gray Firewall is required, the Outer Firewall, Outer Encryption Com-
ponent, Gray Firewall and Inner Encryption Component must use physically separate compo-
nents, such that no component is used for more than one function.
MSC-SR-12
If the MSC Solution uses the Public Internet for its Black transport network, an Outer Firewall
must be located between the Black transport network and the Outer Encryption Component.
MSC-PF-22
If an Outer Firewall is required, for all Outer Firewall interfaces, traffic filtering rules must be
applied to both inbound and outbound traffic, such that only IKE, ESP, MKA, MACsec and
control plane protocols (as defined in this CP) approved by organization-defined policy are
allowed.
MSC-GD-1
All Solution Components, with the exception of the Outer Firewall (if present), must be phys-
ically protected as classified devices, classified at the level of the network with the highest
classification in the solution or in any other MSC Solutions with which it is interconnected.
3.5.2
Outer Encryption Component (OEC)
The Outer Encryption Component (OEC) is located between the Gray Network and the
untrusted Black Network. It acts as the outer boundary of the protected system and is
responsible for applying the first layer of encryption to outgoing data. The OEC can be
implemented as either a VPN Gateway or a MACsec Device, depending on the encryption
method chosen.
The main role of OEC is to establish and manage the outer encryption tunnel using IPsec
or MACsec. This ensures that all traffic crossing the Black Network is protected through
mutual authentication, confidentiality, and data integrity. It must be a physically secured
device (MSC-SR-9). All data passing through the OEC must be encrypted. The device
must never forward unencrypted (plaintext) traffic between the Gray and Black interfaces
(MSC-OR-2). If the OEC is deployed without a separate Outer Firewall, it must perform
its own traffic filtering by enforcing CSfC-specified port and protocol restrictions. This pro-
tects the system from unauthorized access and helps prevent denial-of-service attacks.
Relevant requirements for the Outer Encryption Component are summarized in Table 3.4.
Table 3.4: Outer Encryption Component Requirements
CSfC Req #
Description
MSC-PF-7
Any service or feature that allows an Outer Encryption Component to contact a third-party
server (e.g., one maintained by the manufacturer) must be disabled.
MSC-PF-13
The Gray Network interfaces of Outer Encryption Components must allow IKE and IPsec, or
MKA and MACsec traffic, as appropriate, between two Inner Encryption Components protect-
ing networks of the same security level or being used for management of the Gray Network.
MSC-PF-17
The Gray Network interfaces of Outer Encryption Components must only permit packets whose
source and destination IP addresses match the external interfaces of Inner Encryption Com-
ponents that support Red Networks of the same security level.
MSC-PF-18

source address does not match a list of addresses or address ranges known to be reachable
from the interface where the packet was received.
MSC-OR-3,6

received on an interface connected to a Gray Network from bypassing encryption and being
forwarded out through an interface connected to a Black Network and vice versa.
MSC-OR-4

work, except control plane traffic, must have already been encrypted once.
MSC-OR-7

Outer VPN Gateway
The Outer Encryption Component can be implemented as either a VPN Gateway or a
MACsec device. When configured as a VPN Gateway, it establishes an IPsec tunnel with
its peer device at the remote site, applying the outer layer of encryption as required by
the MSC Capability Package. This IPsec tunnel ensures secure communication over the
untrusted Black Network by providing encryption, authentication, and integrity protection
for all transmitted data. Some VPN Gateway requirements apply for both Outer and Inner
VPN Gateways (Table 3.5). However, the MSC Capability Package also defines specific
requirements that apply only to the Outer VPN Gateway. These are outlined in Table 3.6.

MSC-VG-14
All VPN Gateways must set the ESP SA lifetime to no more than 8 hours.
MSC-VG-16
Before rekeying the IKE SA, each VPN Gateway must re-authenticate the peer’s iden-
tity.
MSC-VG-17
The Mandatory Access Control policy must restrict key access so only the VPN Gate-
way process can access its private key.
MSC-VG-18
All VPN Gateways must use Galois Counter Mode (GCM) for IKE encryption.
Table 3.6: Outer VPN Gateway Requirements
CSfC Req #
Description
MSC-PF-3
For all Outer VPN Gateway interfaces connected to a Black Network, traffic filtering rules must
be applied to both inbound and outbound traffic, such that only IKE, ESP, and control plane
protocols approved by organization-defined policy are allowed.
MSC-PF-10
For solutions using IPv4, Outer VPN Gateways using IPsec must drop all packets that use IP
options.
3.5.3
Gray Firewall
The Gray Firewall is a key security component placed within the Gray Network, positioned
between the Outer and Inner Encryption Components. It is only required when MSC
solutions include multiple Red Networks with different security classifications. If there is
only one Red Network, this component is not mandatory according to the MSC Capability
Package.
The main role of the Gray Firewall is to enforce strict separation between Red Networks
operating at different security levels. It blocks any traffic attempting to pass between Inner
Encryption Components that belong to Red Networks with varying classifications, prevent-
ing the unauthorized flow of sensitive information. In addition, the Gray Firewall helps
control management traffic. It blocks communication from Gray Network management
systems to any Inner Encryption Component that is not part of the highest security-level
Red Network in the architecture.
For security assurance, the Gray Firewall must be treated as a classified and physically
protected device (MSC-GD-1). It must remain physically separate from both the Outer
and Inner Encryption Components (MSC-PS-12). This Capability Package allows the
deployment of multiple Gray Firewalls for redundancy or to improve network performance.
Key requirements for the Gray Firewall are summarized in Table 3.7.

on the CSfC Components List.
MSC-PS-10

come from different manufacturers, where neither manufacturer is a subsidiary of the other;
or be two different products from the same manufacturer, where NSA has determined that the
two products meet the CSfC criteria for implementation independence.

MSC-PF-30

MSC-PF-31

MSC-PF-32
The Gray Firewall must block all traffic routed to and between two or more Inner VPN Gateways
of different classification levels.
MSC-DM-5
The Gray Network must be used exclusively for all management of the Outer Encryption Com-

MSC-DM-25
The Gray Management and Gray Data Networks must be at minimum logically separated by
the Gray Firewall using ACL.
3.5.4
Gray Management Services
Gray Management Services covers the infrastructure responsible for the secure manage-
ment and monitoring of all components operating within the Gray Network. According to
the MSC CP, Gray Management Services typically includes the following components.
• Gray Management Workstation (MW): This component is authorized to configure
and control devices in the Gray Network, such as the Outer Encryption Compo-
nent and the Gray Firewall. It operates on a dedicated Gray Management Network.
Gray Management Workstation is prohibited from managing Inner Encryption Com-
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
17
==PAGE:28==
ponents or any services in the Red Network (MSC-DM-17). The key requirements
for both Gray and Red Management Workstations are listed in Table 3.8.
• Gray Security Information and Event Management (SIEM): The Gray SIEM col-
lects and analyzes encrypted logs and event data from Gray Network components,
including the Outer Encryption Component and Gray Firewall. It helps detect poten-
tial issues and ensure accountability. Gray SIEM is not mandatory in MSC solutions.
More details about the Gray SIEM are provided in Section 3.6.3.
All management-plane traffic must originate on the Gray MW and traverse only the Gray
Management Network. Management sessions and log forwarding are protected end-to-
end using SSHv2, TLS1.2+, IPsec, or MACsec when passing over untrusted segments.
Physical separation of Gray Data and Gray Management Networks is mandatory to pre-
vent any mixing of data-plane and management-plane traffic.
In centralized management setups, the central Gray MW is responsible for managing all
Gray Network components across sites. This approach follows the CSfC Enterprise Gray
Implementation Requirements Annex and helps ensure consistent policy enforcement and
simplified operational control throughout the MSC environment.

3.5.5
Inner Encryption Component (IEC)
The Inner Encryption Component (IEC) provides the first layer of encryption in the CSfC
Multi-Site Connectivity (MSC) dual-encryption architecture. It is positioned between the
Red Network and the Gray Network.
IEC can be implemented as a VPN Gateway or MACsec Device, depending on the chosen

encryption method. It establishes a secure tunnel with a corresponding IEC at a remote
site using standard protocols such as IPsec with ESP or MACsec. This tunnel provides
confidentiality, data integrity, and source authentication, ensuring that only encrypted and
authenticated traffic is permitted between the Red and Gray Networks. Key requirements
for the Inner Encryption Component are listed in Table 3.9.

of encryption by establishing an IPsec tunnel with its peer IEC at the remote site. This
ensures that classified data is protected as it leaves the Red Network. General VPN
Gateway requirements are given in the Section 3.5.2 in Table 3.5. In addition, the MSC
Capability Package defines specific requirements for the Inner VPN Gateway, which are
outlined in Table 3.10.
