3.5.7
Red Management Services
Red Management Services refers to the infrastructure responsible for securely managing
and monitoring all components within the Red Network. These services are essential for
maintaining control over classified systems and ensuring secure operations on the Red
network of the MSC solution. According to the MSC Capability Package, Red Manage-
ment Services typically include the following components:
The Red MW is the trusted system used to configure, monitor, and manage Red Network
components, including the Inner Encryption Component (IEC) and any other Red Network
systems.
• Red Management Workstation (MW): The Red MW is the authorized system used
to configure, monitor, and manage Red Network components, including the Inner
Encryption Component (IEC) and any other Red Network Components. To maintain
strict security boundaries, the Red MW is explicitly prohibited from accessing or
managing Outer Encryption Components or any Gray Management Services (MSC-
DM-17). This enforces the principle of separation of duties. Requirements for Red
and Gray Management Workstations are listed in Table 3.8.
• Red Security Information and Event Management (SIEM): The Red SIEM serves
as the centralized logging and analysis point for encrypted audit and event data
generated by the Inner Encryption Components and other Red Network services. It
helps detect anomalies and maintain auditability. A Red SIEM is not a mandatory
component of the MSC Solution. Details of Red SIEM are given in section 3.6.3.
All management-plane traffic must originate from the Red MW and be routed only through
the Red Management Network. This traffic must be encrypted end-to-end using secure
protocols such as SSHv2, TLS 1.2 or later, IPsec, or MACsec, especially when passing
over untrusted segments. Like in the Gray Network, physical separation between the Red
Data Network and Red Management Network is required
In centralized management deployments, the central Red MW is responsible for managing
all Red Network components across sites. Enterprise Gray Implementation Requirements
Annex does not specifically talk about the Red Network. However, Red Management
requirements are similar to Gray Management.
3.6
Supporting Annexes
The CSfC Multi-Site Connectivity (MSC) Capability Package is supported by several an-
nexes that provide detailed guidance on specific operational and security aspects. These
annexes extend on the core Capability Package by clearly outlining required configura-
tions, operational steps, and security measures for each domain.
20
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:31==
The supporting annexes include a wide range of requirements, and include many require-
ments not relevant to MSC CP and our thesis. In this section, we focus only on the annex
requirements that are directly applicable to the MSC solution. The full list of annex re-
quirements is available in the corresponding annex documents. In the following sections,
we explain each supporting annex and the requirements related to MSC CP.
3.6.1
Key Management and Symmetric Key Management Annexes
Key management is essential for maintaining secure communication within CSfC Multi-
Site Connectivity (MSC) solutions. Effective management of cryptographic keys is critical
for ensuring data confidentiality, integrity, and authentication over untrusted networks.
The CSfC program provides two major supporting documents addressing key manage-
ment: the Key Management Requirements Annex[12] and the Symmetric Key Manage-
ment Requirements Annex[13]. These annexes outline specific processes, roles, and
security controls that govern key lifecycles. In this section, we provide an overview of
both annexes, highlighting the primary requirements relevant to MSC CP.
Certificate-Based Key Management
The Key Management Requirements Annex is a critical part of the CSfC program, espe-
cially for Data-in-Transit (DIT) solutions such as Multi-Site Connectivity (MSC). It speci-
fies detailed requirements for key management, certificate authorities (CAs), public key
infrastructures (PKIs), roles of personnel, and revocation mechanisms to ensure the au-
thenticity, confidentiality, and integrity of classified data. CSfC DIT solutions extensively
use X.509 certificates and asymmetric cryptographic algorithms to authenticate devices
and establish Outer and Inner encryption tunnels.
To meet security requirements, all certificates used in CSfC MSC solutions must adhere
to the following principles:
• Each solution component (e.g. Outer/Inner VPN Gateways) requires A private au-
thentication key.
• All certificates must be Non-Person Entity (NPE) certificates (KM-5), except for spe-
cific Mobile Access (MA) TLS cases where a user certificate might be needed for
the Inner tunnel.
• Certificates must comply with the CNSA Suite profiles defined in RFC 5280 and
RFC 8603 (KM-8).
• Certificate revocation status must be checked using Certificate Revocation Lists
(CRLs) or Online Certificate Status Protocol (OCSP) (KM-CR-1,KM-CR-2, KM-CR-
13)
• CRLs must be issued and signed by the respective CA (KM-CR-19, KM-CR-20).
• All certificates must be used strictly for authentication and never for any other pur-
pose, like encryption or signing (KM-6).
Preferably, public/private key pairs are generated directly on the solution component to
prevent private key export. If this isn’t possible, a dedicated offline management worksta-
tion must be used to create the key pair (KM-CI-4).
A core design principle of the MSC solution is the strict separation between the Outer and
Inner encryption tunnels. According to this principle, Certification Authorities (CAs) are
separated.
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
21
==PAGE:32==
• Outer CA: Issues certificates for Outer Encryption Components and Gray Manage-
ment Services (KM-2, KM-CI-15).
• Inner CA: Issues certificates exclusively for Inner Encryption Components and Red
Management Services (KM-3, KM-CI-14).
Each CA must maintain a separate trust chain (Root CA) to avoid cross-validation between
Outer and Inner certificates (KM-24). MSC solutions can adopt one of two CA deployment
strategies:
• Enterprise CAs are recommended when integrating other CSfC Capability Pack-
ages into the solution.
• Locally operated CAs may offer more control in isolated deployments.
Regardless of the approach, Enterprise CAs must not be reachable over the Black Net-
work. Additionally, Inner CAs must be hosted entirely within the Red Network, while Outer
CAs may be placed in either the Red or Gray Network, based on the overall system design
(KM-16, KM-18).
Symmetric Key Management (Pre-Shared Keys)
The Symmetric Key Management Requirements Annex is a critical supplemental docu-
ment under the Commercial Solutions for Classified (CSfC) Program. It provides manda-
tory requirements for the generation, distribution, use, rekeying, compromise recovery,
and destruction of symmetric Pre-Shared Keys (PSKs) in CSfC solutions.
Generally, PKI-based certificates are preferred for authentication. However, PSK supports
quantum-resistant cryptographic protection in CSfC deployments, addressing the threat
from quantum computing against traditional public-key algorithms. For CSfC solutions that
have a requirement to protect long-life classified information, at least one of the encryption
tunnels must use PSKs. This annex suggests both inner and outer tunnels should utilize
PSKs where possible. However, to ensure robust authentication, at least one tunnel must
use public/private key certificates for mutual authentication (PSK-U-7). When PSKs are
employed, they add a layer for quantum-resistant confidentiality, but public key certificates
must still be used for mutual authentication (PSK-U-8).
Supported protocols for PSK-based encryption:
• IPsec with IKEv2 compliant with RFC 8784 (using Post-Quantum PSKs - PPKs)
• Media Access Control Security (MACsec) (using pre-shared Connectivity Associa-
tion Keys - CAKs)
The annex specifies how PSKs apply to different Capability Packages. For the MSC CP,
the following configurations are supported:
1. Inner and Outer VPN Devices: PSKs should be implemented on both the inner
and outer tunnel VPN Devices with IPsec RFC 8784-compliant IKEv2.
2. Inner VPN Device and Outer MACsec Device: PSKs should be used for the outer
tunnel MACsec Devices and PSKs should be used for the inner tunnel VPN Devices
with IPsec RFC 8784-compliant IKEv2.
3. Inner MACsec Device and Outer VPN Device: PSKs should be used for the inner
tunnel MACsec Devices and PSKs should be used for the outer tunnel VPN Devices
with IPsec RFC 8784-compliant IKEv2.
22
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:33==
4. Inner and Outer MACsec Devices: PSKs should be used for either the Inner tunnel
MACsec Devices or the Outer tunnel MACsec Devices.
Key Generation and Management
The PSK management is handled by a Key Gen-
eration Solution (KGS), which is responsible for generating, distributing, and securely
installing the PSKs.
PSKs must be generated by an NSA-approved Key Generation Solution (KGS) in Red
Network(PSK-KC-1). This KGS must utilize a FIPS 140-2/3 validated or NSA-approved
Random Number Generator (RNG) seeded with at least 256 bits of entropy (PSK-GD-1).
PSKs themselves must be at least 256 bits long (PSK-GD-3). Figure 3.5 in the annex
illustrates how the KGS operates within the Red Network to manage PSK generation for
both Red and Gray network components.
Figure 3.5: Illustration of PSK Management Services, showing how the KGS operates
within the Red Network enclave to manage PSK generation. (retrieved from [13])
Figure 3.6: Secure PSK distribution and installation process, highlighting how the DM
requests, receives, and installs PSKs securely. (retrieved from [13])
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
23
==PAGE:34==
PSK Distribution and Installation
The KGS securely distributes PSKs to authorized
Device Managers (DMs). PSKs must be protected during distribution to prevent plain-
text exposure (PSK-GD-4). This can involve encryption or, if encryption is not feasible,
manual distribution (PSK-GD-14). Manual distribution must adhere strictly to Two-Person
Integrity (TPI) procedures (PSK-GD-5), ensuring no single person has access to the plain-
text key. The Device Manager is responsible for installing the PSKs into CSfC devices
(such as Inner Encryption Components) and ensuring that no plaintext copies of the key
remain after installation. Figure 3.6 in the annex details this secure PSK distribution and
installation process.
Rekeying and Compromise Recovery
PSKs must be rekeyed regularly, with intervals
ranging from 30 to 180 days, depending on data classification levels and threat assess-
ments (PSK-RK-1). This minimizes the exposure window if a key is compromised. Ad-
ditionally, any compromised keys must be promptly replaced without waiting for planned
revocation procedures (PSK-CR-3).
3.6.2
Enterprise Gray Implementation Annex and Centralized
Management
The Enterprise Gray (EG) Implementation Requirements Annex [11] provides detailed
guidance for designing centralized and scalable Commercial Solutions for Classified (CSfC)
networks. The main goal of this annex is to enable secure and centralized management
of geographically distributed Red, Gray, and Black network components while protecting
classified data.
In a centralized management configuration (shown in Figure 3.7), remote sites typically
do not host local management services. Instead, all management functions for remote
components are performed centrally using Gray Management VPNs. All administrative
access must be protected using SSHv2, TLS, or IPsec, with algorithms approved under
the CNSA Suite (EG-FW-15).
Figure 3.7: Centrally Managed MSC Architecture
Components and Central Management Configurations
The EG Annex introduces key modifications to the MSC Capability Package architecture.
A significant update is the introduction and role of the Gray Firewall/Encryption Compo-
nent. This component is central to the EG architecture, provides several critical security
and networking functions to enable secure, scalable, and centrally managed deployments.
• Firewall and Traffic Segregation: It acts as a robust firewall, enforcing strict policy
controls. A crucial function is filtering traffic to ensure that Inner Encryption Com-
24
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:35==
ponents serving different classification levels cannot communicate with each other
(EG-FW-5), as illustrated in Figure 3.8.
Figure 3.8: Multiple Inner Classification Prohibited Traffic (retrieved from [11])
• Encryption Gateway: The Outer Encryption Component provides the outer layer,
and the Gray Firewall/Encryption Component provides the inner layer by establish-
ing secure Gray Management VPNs. This supports secure management across
untrusted links. It leverages the same Certificate Authority (CA) as the Outer En-
cryption Component and the Gray Management Network. If enhanced security is
required, a dedicated CA or Pre-Shared Key (PSK) authentication may be imple-
mented instead (EG-AR-12, 13).
• Dynamic Routing: To support scalability across distributed sites, it enables rout-
ing via approved protocols including BGP, OSPF, and RIPv2 (EG-AR-22). Dynamic
routing is only allowed on the Gray Firewall/Encryption Component; no other de-
vices on the network can perform dynamic routing (EG-DR-1). If dynamic routing
protocols are used, routes must not be shared between the Gray Management and
Data Tunnels (EG-DR-11).
• VRF Implementation: This component also handles mandatory Virtual Routing and
Forwarding (VRF) to separate Management and Data planes(EG-AR-16).
For centralized management configuration, additional modifications are applied to the
MSC Capability Package components. The Gray Administration Workstation (MW) is
configured to manage remote components, while the Gray SIEM is set up to collect logs
from remote Monitoring Points.
Although the Enterprise Gray Annex does not extensively detail Red components, the
principle of centralizing management functions applies to them as well.
Scalability and Survivability
Scalability and Availability are critical concerns of this thesis and important aspects of
Multi-Site Connectivity (MSC) solutions. Enterprise Gray (EG) architecture with central-
ized management provides scalability and survivability features to CSfC Solutions.
Static routing is commonly used between VPN components. However, as the number of
remote sites grows, static routing becomes increasingly inefficient and prone to miscon-
figuration. To address this, the EG Annex introduces the use of dynamic routing protocols
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
25
==PAGE:36==
within CSfC Solutions. Dynamic routing is permitted only on Gray Firewalls and Gray En-
cryption Components. Other components are not permitted to perform routing functions
in the MSC architecture (EG-DR-1).
To support routing scalability while maintaining logical separation, the annex also requires
the use of Virtual Routing and Forwarding (VRF) when dynamic routing is enabled. Two
separate VRFs must be implemented: one for Gray Management traffic and one for Gray
Data traffic (EG-AR-16). Each VRF must contain only relevant route information (EG-AR-
18, EG-AR-19). This design enforces a strict boundary between management and data
planes, supporting secure and scalable expansion of the Enterprise Gray network.
While centralized management improves operational control, it also introduces a single
point of failure. To improve survivability, each remote site must include its own authen-
tication server to validate EUDs and encryption components independently (EG-AR-23).
This ensures that basic network services and tunnels can be re-established even if central
connectivity is lost.
Other than these requirements, there is an extensive list of requirements given in Enter-
prise Gray Requirements Annex [11]. Relevant requirements with MSC CP are provided
in Appendix A.1.
3.6.3
Continuous Monitoring Annex
CSfC Continuous Monitoring (CM) Annex [14] provides guidance and requirements to
ensure ongoing security monitoring within CSfC-compliant solutions. It defines the ar-
chitecture, processes, and tools needed to continuously monitor and respond to threats
across Red, Gray, and Black networks.
In this section, we highlight key concepts and requirements from the CM Annex related
to Multi-Site Connectivity (MSC) solutions. CM Annex provides a comprehensive list of
requirements. This section only mentions relevant requirements and the rest of the re-
quirements can be found in Continuous Monitoring Annex [14].
CM plays a critical role in CSfC Solutions by:
• Establishing a baseline for expected system and network behaviour
• Detecting misconfigured components to maintain assurance for protecting classified
data in transit
• Analyzing system activity to identify unauthorized actions and security anomalies
Figure 3.9: Continuous Monitoring Solution – Multi-Site Connectivity CP
26
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:37==
The CM architecture collects logs from Monitoring Points (MPs) across the Red, Gray, and
Black networks. These MPs feed data into Security Information and Event Management
(SIEM) systems. This system enables real-time visibility, threat detection, and incident
response capabilities. Data for the CM solution can originate from sources such as Net-
work Taps, Intrusion Detection/Prevention Systems (IDS/IPS), and system logs. Figure
3.9 illustrates a deployment of Continuous Monitoring within an MSC solution.
Security Information and Event Management (SIEM)
Figure 3.10: Gray SIEM showing log traffic
SIEM systems are central to the Continuous Monitoring architecture. They collect, cor-
relate, and generate alerts based on security-relevant events collected from across the
network.
• A Gray SIEM collects logs from Gray Network components, including Outer VPN
Gateways, Gray Firewalls, Authentication Servers, and Gray Monitoring Points (e.g.,
MP6). he Gray SIEM must receive all system logs and network monitoring data
collected from the MPs within Gray network (CM-SM-3).
• A Red SIEM performs a similar role on the Red Network, gathering logs from com-
ponents such as Inner VPN Gateways, Red Authentication Servers, and Red Moni-
toring Points (e.g., MP7). It must receive all relevant logs and monitoring data from
the Red network (CM-SM-4).
Each SIEM must monitor misconfigurations, unauthorized access attempts, policy vio-
lations, unexpected tunnel behaviour, and certificate status issues. Collected logs and
SIEM notifications must be reviewed by security administrators at regular intervals defined
by the Authorizing Official (AO) or relevant policies, but at least once a week (CM-LN-18).
All logs forwarded to a SIEM or collection server should be encrypted using robust proto-
cols such as SSHv2, IPsec, or TLS 1.2 or later (CM-LN-17). These collected logs must
be maintained for a minimum of 1 year (MSC-GD-13).
In centralized deployments, SIEM logs from remote sites should be transmitted over the
dedicated management network back to the central site, as illustrated in Figure 3.10.
Specific SIEM requirements are detailed in Table A.5 and A.6 in Appendix A.2.
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
27
==PAGE:38==
Monitoring Points
In CSfC Multi-Site Connectivity (MSC) solutions, monitoring must be implemented across
Red, Gray, and optionally Black networks. This ensures full visibility over classified traf-
fic and system behaviour. The Continuous Monitoring (CM) Annex defines 8 conceptual
Monitoring Points (MP1–MP8), each representing a strategic location for deploying mon-
itoring capabilities. The required and optional monitoring points are summarized in Table
3.11.
Table 3.11: Monitoring Points for MSC Solutions
MP #
Location
Required?
MP6
Gray Management Network
Required (CM-MP-1)
MP7
Red Management Network
Required (CM-MP-1)
MP1/MP2/MP3
Gray/Black Network (Data Plane)
At least one required (CM-MP-3)
MP4/MP5
Red Network (Data Plane)
At least one required (CM-MP-5)
MP8
End User Devices (EUDs)
Optional
In the following sections, we briefly summarize the roles of each Monitoring Point and
highlight selected key requirements. The Continuous Monitoring Annex provides an ex-
tensive and detailed list of requirements for each Monitoring Point individually. Due to the
volume and specificity of these requirements, including all of them even in an appendix
is not practical. Therefore, we only reference the most relevant requirements here. The
full list of requirements can be found in the Continuous Monitoring Annex [14] for compre-
hensive coverage.
MP6 – Gray Management Monitoring Point
MP6 is a mandatory monitoring point for MSC solutions (CM-MP-1). It is deployed in
the Gray Management Network and is responsible for monitoring management-related
activity and logs. Data collected can range from system logs and network flow data to full
packet captures via taps or port mirroring.
This monitoring point captures essential events such as administrative logins and failed
login attempts (CM-MP6-1), user privilege changes (CM-MP6-5), and modifications to
configuration or access control policies (CM-MP6-6, CM-MP6-7). It also monitors the
establishment and termination of IPsec tunnels (CM-MP6-17, CM-MP6-18).
All logs collected at MP6 must be securely transmitted to the Gray SIEM using SSHv2,
TLS 1.2+, or IPsec (CM-LN-17).
MP7 – Red Management Monitoring Point
Similar to MP6, MP7 is a mandatory monitoring point for all CSfC solutions (CM-MP-1),
located within the Red Management Network. It focuses on the administrative traffic and
operational status of Red Network components, including Inner Encryption Component(s),
Inner Firewall(s), Red Authentication Services, and the Red SIEM.
MP7 has similar requirements to MP6. This monitoring point captures essential events
such as administrative logins and failed login attempts (CM-MP7-1), user privilege changes
(CM-MP7-5), and modifications to configuration or access control policies (CM-MP7-6,
MP7-7). It also monitors the establishment and termination of IPsec tunnels (CM-MP7-
17, MP7-18). It ensures that all certificate-based authentication events, administrative
actions, and configuration updates in the Red domain are logged and securely transmit-
ted to the Red SIEM. Like MP6, logs must be securely forwarded to the Red SIEM and
retained locally in case of central SIEM outages (CM-MS-2). MP7 plays a crucial role in
ensuring accountability and visibility across Red Management Network operations.
28
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:39==
MP1 / MP2 / MP3 – Gray or Black Data Monitoring Point
MSC solutions must deploy at least one monitoring point from the MP1, MP2, or MP3
group to inspect traffic flowing into the Gray Network (CM-MP-3). These MPs provide
visibility into potential threats or protocol violations on the data plane between the Black
and Gray networks.
• MP1 (optional in MSC): It is located in the Black Network. It captures raw Black
Network traffic. It monitors traffic that has passed the outermost perimeter defense.
This makes it useful for detecting reconnaissance, denial-of-service attempts, or
unapproved packet types.
• MP2 (most commonly used in MSC): Located between the Outer Firewall and the
Outer VPN Gateway. It observes IPsec ESP or TLS traffic. MP2 detects unau-
thorized port access (CM-MP2-4), scanning activity (CM-MP2-5), and anomalies in
expected protocol behaviour.
• MP3 (used if needed): It is deployed between the Gray Firewall and the Outer En-
cryption Component. It monitors tunnelled traffic before it enters the Gray Data
Network.
Implementing one of these MPs allows the solution owner to ensure that only properly
encrypted and authenticated traffic is processed by Gray Network components.
MP4 / MP5 – Red Data Monitoring Point
At least one of MP4 or MP5 is required in MSC implementations to observe classified data
traffic at the Red Network (CM-MP-5). These monitoring points provide insight into data
flows in trusted Red Networks. At the Monitoring Points, the data is in unencrypted form.
That’s why deep packet inspection can be utilized at these monitoring points if necessary.
• MP4 is located between the Red Firewall and the Inner Encryption Component. MP4
plays a key role in security by logging any unusual data movement within or out of
this network segment (CM-MP4-1). Furthermore, it logs any attempts from within
the Red Network at this point to connect to external, unauthorized domains or IP
addresses (CM-MP4-2).
• MP5 is also in the Red Network, but it monitors the data line inside the Inner Fire-
wall. It monitors the most secure network traffic that has passed all CSfC network
security layers. The MP5 is important for logging unusual data movements at this
final checkpoint before data reaches end resources, providing a last opportunity to
detect anomalies (CM-MP5-1).
These Red Data Monitoring Points, MP4 and MP5, are therefore essential for maintaining
the security integrity of the Red Network. Both MP4 and MP5 are mandated to log when
a system generates/receives an excessive number of packets. This can be an indicator
of certain types of network anomalies or reconnaissance activities (CM-MP4-3, CM-MP5-
3). Additionally, both monitoring points log any protocol or port being used that is outside
the allowed by the Inner Firewall and/or Inner Encryption Component (CM-MP4-5, CM-
MP5-5). This ensures that all network activity adheres strictly to defined policies and the
network baseline.
MP8 – End User Device Monitoring Point
MP8 focuses on monitoring activities directly on the End User Devices (EUDs). While it
is required in some other CSfC Capability Packages [16], it is optional for solutions based
on the MSC Capability Package. When deployed, MP8 collects system logs, application
events, and security alerts from user devices. This includes data on login attempts, con-
figuration changes, unauthorized software installations, and potential malware activity.
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
29
==PAGE:40==
MP8 is especially valuable in MSC deployments with central management sites where
many users interact with Red Network services. The data is gathered in Red SIEM. It
helps enhance visibility and supports forensic investigations during security incidents.
3.7
Summary and Key Contributions
In this chapter, we provided a comprehensive overview of the CSfC Multi-Site Connec-
tivity (MSC) Capability Package and its supporting annexes. With this exploration, we
established a theoretical background for understanding secure multi-site connectivity in
classified environments. We outlined the MSC CP’s high-level design (Section 3.2), which
implements a dual-layer encryption strategy to protect classified data across untrusted
networks. A significant part of the chapter focused on the core components (Section 3.5)
of an MSC solution.
Furthermore, we summarized key aspects of supporting annexes (Section 3.6). This in-
cluded an overview of the Key Management annexes (Section 3.6.1) regarding the secure
handling of cryptographic keys, the Enterprise Gray annex (Section 3.6.2) for centralized
management and scalability, and the Continuous Monitoring annex (Section 3.6.3) con-
cerning ongoing security assurance through SIEM systems and Monitoring Points.
Understanding this chapter is significant for the rest of the study because it provides the
fundamentals of this thesis.
RQ2: What are the core requirements, security principles, and architectural com-
ponents of the CSfC MSC Capability Package?
We detailed the core requirements by analyzing the MSC CP and its annexes. We ex-
plained the necessary configurations for each major component (Section 3.5) and man-
agement services.
We outlined fundamental security principles, including defense-in-depth through dual en-
cryption (Sections 3.2, 3.3), clear network segmentation (Red, Gray, Black networks, as
explained in Section 3.4), and the separation of management and data traffic (Section
3.4, Section 3.2.1).
We described the architectural components (Section 3.5), highlighting their roles within
the different network layers and how they work together to ensure secure connections.
RQ3: What are the key advantages, implementation challenges, and trade-offs of
designing and deploying a secure multi-site connectivity solution based on the
CSfC MSC Capability Package?
We highlighted advantages, such as the strong, layered security offered by dual encryp-
tion (Sections 3.2, 3.3) and the flexibility of using commercial off-the-shelf (COTS) prod-
ucts (Section 3.1).
We explained implementation challenges, including the complexity of setting up and man-
aging two separate encryption tunnels (Sections 3.5.2, 3.5.5), the requirement for diverse
components to reduce shared vulnerabilities, and the detailed planning needed for key
management (Section 3.6.1) and continuous monitoring (Section 3.6.3).
30
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:41==
4
Literature Review
In this chapter, we present an overview of both commercial offerings and open-source
alternative solutions relevant to secure multi-site connectivity. Our main goal is to explore
the tools and technologies available for secure multi-site connectivity and evaluate how
well they meet the requirements of the CSfC Multi-Site Connectivity Capability Package
(MSC CP). We begin by exploring CSfC-approved commercial solutions. Then we have
a literature review of open-source alternatives.
4.1
Commercial Solutions
The CSfC program mandates strict validation of solution components to ensure they meet
stringent security standards. This often involves evaluation with the Common Criteria
and adherence to National Information Assurance Partnership (NIAP)-approved protec-
tion profiles. This ensures that approved components meet the high security standards for
classified communication. The NSA maintains a list of Commercial-Off-The-Shelf (COTS)
products that meet these standards and are authorized for use in CSfC solutions[15].
Utilizing CSfC-approved commercial products offers several advantages. These compo-
nents are formally certified under standards such as Common Criteria and FIPS, ensuring
a high level of trust and assurance. In addition, they are typically supported by vendor-
provided Service Level Agreements (SLAs) and technical support [17]. These supports
simplifies deployment and ensures security compliance and operational reliability. While
security is the primary focus, many premium COTS solutions also deliver strong perfor-
mance. For example, the Juniper SRX5400 supports throughput up to 1 Tbps [18].
Despite these benefits, there are some important limitations of CSfC-approved compo-
nents. Total Cost of Ownership (TCO) of these components is often high due to licensing
fees, hardware costs, and support agreements [19]. Second, many of these solutions lead
to vendor lock-in [20]. When an organization invests deeply in one vendor’s proprietary
hardware, management interfaces, and personnel certifications, switching to alternative
solutions becomes costly and complex. This reduces long-term architectural flexibility.
The list of CSfC-approved commercial products covers a wide range of vendors and tech-
nologies. In the following sections, we highlight key vendors and components that are
relevant for implementing the Multi-Site Connectivity (MSC) Capability Package.
Cisco Systems – IPsec VPN Gateways and Firewalls
Cisco offers a wide range of CSfC-approved devices. Some of the approved products
are ruggedized options like the Catalyst IR8300 [21], alongside high-performance virtual
(Catalyst 8000V [22]) and physical routers (ISR 1000 [23], ESR 6300 [24]). These so-
lutions are widely deployed due to their high scalability, support for strong cryptographic
algorithms (IKEv2, AES-256), and compliance with the Network Device Protection Profile
(NDcPP). They provide strong throughput options ranging from several hundred Mbps
up to multi-gigabit [23, 22], and are often deployed in redundant site-to-site VPN archi-
tectures. However, they require significant configuration expertise and licensing costs,
which may limit accessibility for smaller organizations.
Juniper Networks – SRX Series and vSRX
Juniper SRX firewalls[25] (hardware) and vSRX [26] (virtual) are widely deployed CSfC-
approved solutions. These firewalls combine IPsec VPN, intrusion prevention, and ad-
vanced routing features in one appliance. The product line scales from small branch
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
31
==PAGE:42==
devices (e.g., SRX300) to powerful data center-grade models (e.g., SRX5800). Juniper
devices are known for their reliable performance, even when handling heavy traffic, es-
pecially encrypted data. Their full support for CNSA Suite and NIAP certification makes
them a strong fit for environments where compliance and performance are equally critical.
However, like Cisco, deployment complexity can be a challenge.
Palo Alto Networks – PA-Series and VM-Series Firewalls
Palo Alto Networks offers both physical (e.g., PA-440 to PA-7000 series[27]) and virtual
(VM-Series [28]) firewalls with CSfC certification. These devices provide high IPsec VPN
throughput, support CNSA algorithms, and integrate advanced threat prevention. Palo
Alto also promotes unified management and a zero-trust security model, making their
solutions a good fit for securing complex, distributed multi-site networks.
HPE Aruba – Mobility Controllers with VPN Capabilities
Aruba’s Mobility Controllers (e.g., 9004[29], 7240XM[30]) support IPsec VPN alongside
enterprise wireless management. These devices are NIAP and CSfC approved when
running ArubaOS 8.10 or later and are suitable for environments combining Wi-Fi with
secure tunnel requirements. While Aruba is mainly known for wireless LAN management,
these NIAP-certified devices offer a combined solution ideal for environments where both
secure Wi-Fi access and site-to-site VPN tunnels are prerequisites.

4.2
Open-Source Tools
Open-source solutions have gained considerable traction due to their transparency, cost-
efficiency, and high level of customization capabilities [41]. Despite these advantages,
using open-source solutions in secure environments comes with challenges, particularly
when trying to meet the strict security, performance, and reliability standards.
Although the CSfC Multi-Site Connectivity (MSC) Capability Package usually relies on
certified commercial solutions, this thesis examines whether open-source alternatives
can meet similar requirements. Specifically, we evaluate how well these tools can re-
place the core Commercial Off-The-Shelf (COTS) components defined in the MSC CP
framework. This section provides a review of available open-source solutions, focusing
on their capabilities, security features, and performance characteristics in classified multi-
site connectivity scenarios. Unlike commercial CSfC-approved products, open-source
tools lack formal validation. However, they offer strong advantages in terms of flexibility,
adaptability, and lower cost. This makes them viable alternatives.
There are various open-source tools available. We’ve established several practical criteria
to determine which tools to include in our discussion.
• Fully Open-Source
• Actively Supported and Maintained
• Must support key features relevant to the CSfC MSC framework
• Satisfies performance and security requirements for confidential environments
4.2.1
Open-source Firewall and VPN Gateway Solutions
Robust VPN Gateways solutions and Firewalls are critical for establishing secure multi-
site connectivity. MSC CP requires a separation of firewall and encryption components
(MSC-PS-11, MSC-PS-12). However, the use of dedicated Gray or Outer Firewalls is not
mandatory in all cases, as outlined in Sections 3.5.1 and 3.5.3. Based on these guidelines,
our system architecture does not require separate firewall devices. We design our solution
architecture accordingly as detailed in Chapter 6.
MSC CP also mentioned that encryption components may also provide robust firewall
capabilities if a separate firewall device does not exist (see [10, page 16]). In this literature
review, we therefore focus on open-source tools that combine VPN gateway and firewall
functionalities, enabling both secure tunnelling and basic access control within a single
component.
Open-source firewalls can offer substantial cost savings by eliminating initial and opera-
tional expenses and allowing the use of commodity hardware or virtualized environments.
Beyond cost, they provide flexibility for customization and transparency through publicly
accessible source code. Also, the users can integrate other open-source tools and plat-
forms into the solutions without waiting for vendor features[42]. These solutions generally
use open-source operating systems such as Linux, FreeBSD, and OpenBSD because
they offer extensive built-in networking and security features [43].
However, the main drawback of open-source tools is a lack of formal certification. They do
not have Common Criteria Evaluation or FIPS 140 validation for the distributions them-
selves, which is required for CSfC Approved Components. Additionally, deploying and
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
33
==PAGE:44==
maintaining these tools demands a high level of technical expertise. They often lack the
polished interfaces and vendor support found in commercial products, which may pose a
challenge for organizations without in-house expertise [44].
As part of this review, we evaluated a wide range of open-source tools. We excluded
some of them based on our defined criteria:
• IPCOP, Smoothwall, Monowall, Tomato, ZeroShell, DD-WRT – These solutions are
no longer actively maintained or updated, making them unsuitable due to the re-
quirement for active security updates and ongoing development.
• Untangle, Sophos, Endian, VyOS, MikroTik RouterOS – While these solutions have
open-source components, they are not fully free to use, often requiring paid licenses
or subscriptions for full functionality, which does not meet the requirement for com-
pletely open-source and free-to-use software.
In this analysis, we focus on OPNsense, pfSense, VyOS, IPFire, and OpenWrt, which
can serve as firewall/VPN gateways. We review these tools in the following sections for
their ability to serve as a firewall and VPN gateway components in an open-source MSC-
compliant architecture.
pfSense
PfSense is a FreeBSD-based open-source firewall and router distribution that provides
enterprise-level security features [45]. It delivers robust core routing and stateful firewall
capabilities, making it a suitable candidate for combined deployment as a network security
gateway in diverse environments [46]. Even if pfSense is a free, open-source tool, it is
supported and maintained by Netgate [47]. However, there is extensive community-driven
support available.
PfSense provides support for multiple VPN protocols, including IPsec, OpenVPN, L2TP
and WireGuard[48]. PfSense also provides other critical security features. pfSense sup-
ports IDS/IPS through installable packages, The platform includes system monitoring
tools that provide access to logs and enable traffic monitoring. Security is enforced via a
Stateful Packet Inspection (SPI) firewall. Pfsense has a flexible update cycle, generally
driven by significant feature additions or bug fixes.
VPN performance of pfSense varies by protocol. IPsec and WireGuard generally offer
the highest throughput. OpenVPN performance in pfSense can be significantly slower.
Users reported that WireGuard on pfSense can achieve around 600 Mbps [49].
pfSense is not CSfC certified as a product, but its components can be configured to align
with CSfC requirements [45]. For example, pfSense’s IPsec supports NSA Suite B cryp-
tography options like AES-GCM and elliptic-curve DH groups.
OPNsense
OPNsense is built upon the FreeBSD operating system and serves as a comprehensive
routing and firewall platform. It provides robust core routing capabilities and a stateful
firewall, making it well-suited for combined deployment as a primary network security
gateway[50]. OPNSense is a fork of pfSense, so it provides similar functionalities to pf-
Sense.
Unlike pfSense, OPNsense comes with integrated tools such as Suricata and WireGuard.
It does not require subsequent installation with the package manager like pfSense.
The recommended hardware specifications for running all standard features in most use
cases include a 1.5 GHz multi-core CPU and at least 4 GB of RAM[51]. OPNSense has
34
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:45==
similar performance to pfSense on WireGuard, which can achieve 530 Mbps. However,
this throughput performance may vary with hardware specifications and VPN tunnel con-
figuration setups.
OPNsense has an active community and commercial support from Deciso (the developer
company). This ensures timely security updates[50].
OPNsense is not a CSfC-certified product. However, its components and features can be
configured to align with CSfC principles. Its robust VPN capabilities (IPsec, OpenVPN,
WireGuard) are fundamental for implementing the dual-tunnel architecture required by the
MSC-CP-7.
VyOS
VyOS is a Debian GNU/Linux-based open-source network operating system, specifically
designed as a versatile routing and firewall platform. It offers extensive core routing func-
tionalities, supporting advanced protocols such as BGP, OSPF and VRF. VyOS also im-
plements a stateful firewall and zone-based firewall policies, making it highly suitable for
combined deployment as a secure network gateway[52]. VyOS offers enterprise-grade
routing features and is widely used by large organizations [53].
The core VyOS system is open source with executable binaries. However, pre-built pack-
ages and VM images are paid-support products to be used. Unlike pfSense and OP-
NSense, VyOS is not supported by the company. But they are supported by multiple
foundations and provide paid licenses to continue support [54].
VyOS is utilized in ruggedized hardware solutions targeted at military and defense appli-
cations. The Cloudberry MIL CR-6228 Series incorporates VyOS and claims NIAP, CSfC,
and Common Criteria certifications [55].
IPFire
IPFire is a security-focused open-source firewall based on Linux. It incorporates a modu-
lar design, dividing network interfaces into color-coded zones (Green, Red, Orange, Blue)
to simplify firewall policy for different segments (LAN, WAN, DMZ, WiFi)[56]. IPFire does
not provide a Web GUI, and management is done via CLI and API solutions.
IPFire also provides security features like IDS/IPS, VPN Support, Logging Capabilities,
Access Control Mechanisms with a stateful firewall.
IPFire is described as a ”fast” firewall solution, reportedly capable of handling bandwidth
up to 10 Gbit/s in a data center environment[56]. However, IPFire is considered for non-
confidential small businesses and performance-focused firewalling scenarios.
IPFire primarily relies on community support, including active forums and detailed docu-
mentation available through the project’s official wiki. For organizations that require pro-
fessional assistance, Lightning Wire Labs offers commercial support services for IPFire
[57]. However, this support does not include formal patch management or ongoing main-
tenance services. Like many other open-source tools, IPFire also lacks formal certifica-
tions such as Common Criteria or FIPS 140 validation.
OpenWrt
OpenWRT is a highly extensible GNU/Linux distribution designed primarily for embedded
devices, particularly wireless routers. OpenWRT provides extensive core routing capa-
bilities and a stateful firewall based on Linux Netfilter/iptables[58].
Due to its modular structure, OpenWRT does not provide some built-in security features.
OpenWRT is designed for low-power devices and enables custom configurations [58].
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
35
==PAGE:46==
That’s why it does not include all the features. IDS/IPS and some other capabilities can be
added by installing packages. It provides basic Log mechanisms but does not include any
logging/monitoring tools. Similarly, Web GUI is not provided inherently and LuCI package
is required to allow Web GUI-based management[58]. Similar to the other open-source
tools, OpenWRT does not provide certifications.
Comparison of Open-Source Firewalls

its strengths and limitations. Selecting the most appropriate solution requires a careful
assessment of specific needs, along with a comparison of key features, performance,
security capabilities, and overall suitability for the intended deployment environment. We
provided a side-by-side comparison of the core characteristics of the leading open-source
firewall platforms discussed in this review in Table 4.2.

As a result of the review, we have the following insights on open-source firewall and VPN
Gateway solutions:
• No Direct CSfC Certification: A primary finding is that while these open-source so-
lutions offer robust security features, none are formally CSfC-certified products.
• Support for VPN Protocols: All solutions support IPsec, OpenVPN, WireGuard.
That’s why it is not added to the table.
• Performance and Hardware Dependency: The performance of any open-source
firewall solution is heavily dependent on the underlying hardware and the specific
configuration, including the types of services enabled.
• Expertise Required: Successfully deploying and maintaining these open-source so-
lutions demands a significant level of technical expertise in networking, security, and
36
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:47==
the specific platform chosen.
• Strong Feature Sets: OPNsense, pfSense and VyOS provide comprehensive fea-
ture sets that are better than many commercial offerings, including advanced fire-
walling, routing, and extensive VPN support.
• VyOS for Advanced Routing: VyOS stands out for its powerful, enterprise-grade
routing capabilities, making it an excellent choice where complex routing scenarios
are combined with firewall and VPN needs. However, Long-Term Support (LTS)
versions from source require paid support.
Several studies offer insights into the performance and security characteristics of OP-
Nsense and pfSense. OPNsense is often praised for its user-friendly interface and strong
community support, making it a good choice for smaller organizations or those prioritizing
ease of management. In contrast, pfSense is recognized for its robust performance and
extensive customization options, which may appeal more to larger enterprises or those
with very specific configuration needs [59, 60].
Performance benchmarks vary based on study and configuration. Some studies suggest
pfSense may outperform OPNsense and others in throughput and latency under certain
conditions [59, 61]. These findings suggest that while both platforms are strong candi-
dates, the best choice ultimately depends on the specific priorities and use case. How-
ever, the existing literature lacks a comprehensive, side-by-side analysis of these tools in
the context of CSfC MSC requirements. To address this gap, we present our experimental
evaluation in Section 8.1, where we assess selected open-source solutions based on the
MSC CP criteria and the specific needs defined by DDIS.
4.2.2
VPN Protocols
Virtual Private Networks (VPNs) have a critical role in facilitating secure communica-
tion over potentially untrusted networks. Among open-source VPN technologies, IPsec,
OpenVPN, and WireGuard have gained widespread adoption due to their strong security
features and reliable performance [62].
In this review, we focus on specifically on these three protocols, as they are the most rele-
vant for building secure and high-performance connectivity in CSfC-aligned architectures.
Their comparative characteristics are presented in Table 4.3.
While other VPN protocols such as MPLS, PPTP, and L2TP exist, they generally offer
weaker security guarantees or lower performance and are therefore not included in the
primary comparison. These alternative protocols are briefly discussed in Section 4.2.2.
IPsec - Strongswan
Internet Protocol Security (IPsec) is a mature and widely adopted suite of protocols used
to secure Internet Protocol (IP) communications by authenticating and encrypting each
IP packet of a communication session. IPsec operates at the network layer (Layer 3),
providing security services such as encryption, data integrity, and peer authentication.
IPSec includes key components of Authentication Header (AH), Encapsulating Security
Payload (ESP) and Internet Key Exchange (IKE)[63].
StrongSwan is a widely used open-source IPsec implementation for Linux and other Unix-
based systems. It supports both IKEv1 and IKEv2, but CSfC MSC guidelines specifically
mandate the use of IKEv2 for all VPN gateways (MSC-VG-9) [64].
IPsec can operate in two modes:
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
37
==PAGE:48==
• Transport Mode: Encrypts only the payload of the IP packet, leaving the original
IP header intact. Typically used for host-to-host security[64].
• Tunnel Mode: Encapsulates the entire original IP packet within a new IP packet,
protecting both the payload and the original IP headers. This mode is essential for
creating VPN gateways that connect entire networks, as required by the MSC-IR-1.

IPsec is one of the two explicitly approved technologies for creating encrypted tunnels
in the CSfC MSC CP[10]. As such, IPsec plays a central role in the implementation of
secure, CSfC-compliant multi-site connectivity, and StrongSwan provides a reliable open-
source option for its deployment.
Security Features: StrongSwan supports a wide array of cryptographic algorithms for
IKE and IPsec SAs. It can be configured to adhere to the CNSA Suite as required by
MSC CP as given in Table 3.1.
• Confidentiality (Encryption): StrongSwan supports various encryption algorithms,
including AES (AES-CBC, AES-CTR, AES-GCM), 3DES, Camellia, Blowfish, CAST,
and ChaCha20-Poly1305 (via plugin) [65]. Among these, AES-GCM modes are
generally preferred for their performance and built-in authentication.
• Authentication (Digital Signature): StrongSwan supports RSA (3072-bit) and ECDSA
38
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:49==
(P-384) with SHA-384 hashing [65].
• Integrity (Hashing) and Pseudo-Random Functions (PRF): It includes hashing
algorithms like HMAC-SHA1 and HMAC-SHA2 (SHA-256, SHA-384, SHA-512),
along with AES-XCBC for both PRF and integrity checks [66].
• Key Exchange/Establishment: StrongSwan supports multiple key exchange meth-
ods, including elliptic curve groups (e.g., NIST P-256, P-384, P-521) and traditional
Diffie-Hellman groups (e.g., 1024, 2048, 3072, 4096-bit) [67].
• FIPS 140-2/3 Validation: While StrongSwan itself is not FIPS-validated as a stan-
dalone product, several distributions incorporating it have achieved FIPS 140-2 or
FIPS 140-3 validation. For example, the ”Ubuntu 18.04 StrongSwan Cryptographic
Module” has been validated under FIPS 140-2 [68], and StrongSwan 5.9.5 is listed
as part of a FIPS 140-3 validated cryptographic module on Ubuntu 22.04 LTS [69].
Performance Analysis: The performance of strongSwan IPsec is highly dependent on
system hardware, cryptographic algorithm choices, and configuration parameters. There-
fore, it’s difficult to provide universal performance results.
However, different bench-
marks are available. On high-end hardware (e.g., dual-socket 28-core Intel 4th Gen Xeon
servers with 100 Gbit Intel E810 NICs), IPsec configured with AES-GCM encryption has
achieved throughput of up to 6 Gbit/s [70]. These results highlight the importance of hard-
ware acceleration and tuning for optimal performance.
While providing high-security standards, IPsec’s complexity in configuration and potential
performance bottlenecks during the initial key establishment can be seen as limitations
compared to WireGuard and OpenVPN [71].
OpenVPN
OpenVPN is a widely used open-source VPN solution that establishes secure point-to-
point or site-to-site connections using a custom protocol built on top of SSL/TLS [72].
A key characteristic of OpenVPN is its high degree of configurability, supporting a wide
array of encryption algorithms, authentication methods [73]. OpenVPN can run over UDP
(preferred for performance) or TCP. Running VPNs over TCP can lead to ”TCP meltdown”
issues and is generally avoided unless UDP is blocked [74].
Unlike IPsec, which operates at the network layer, OpenVPN functions primarily at the
transport layer (Layer 4) of the OSI model. It relies heavily on the OpenSSL library for
cryptographic operations, including key exchange, encryption, and hashing [75, 73].
Security Features: OpenVPN’s security model is built around SSL/TLS protocols, with
cryptographic strength derived from the OpenSSL library [76]. It supports a broad range
of symmetric ciphers (e.g., AES-128, AES-192, AES-256 in modes like CBC and GCM),
hash algorithms (e.g., SHA-256, SHA-384, SHA-512), and key exchange mechanisms
(RSA, DH, ECDH, ECDSA) available in OpenSSL. For authentication, OpenVPN com-
monly uses X.509 certificates based on a Public Key Infrastructure (PKI). This approach
aligns well with CSfC guidelines, which recommend certificate-based mutual authentica-
tion for VPN gateways [77].
Performance Analysis: OpenVPN’s performance is influenced by several factors, in-
cluding CPU resources (as encryption is CPU-intensive), the chosen cipher suite, and
the underlying transport protocol. In general, UDP provides better throughput than TCP.
Compared to IPsec and WireGuard, OpenVPN is generally less efficient, especially in
high-throughput scenarios [78, 76]. In comparative tests by NordVPN, OpenVPN speeds
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
39
==PAGE:50==
were significantly lower than WireGuard [79].
WireGuard
WireGuard is a modern, open-source VPN protocol designed to prioritize simplicity, speed,
and strong cryptographic security [78]. Compared to legacy VPN protocols like IPsec and
OpenVPN, WireGuard aims to be significantly faster, leaner, and easier to configure.
WireGuard operates by encapsulating IP packets securely over UDP, functioning primar-
ily at the network layer (Layer 3) while using Layer 4 (UDP) for transport [80, 81]. Its Linux
kernel implementation is built on a minimalist codebase of around 4,000 lines, which im-
proves both performance and security through ease of auditing and reduced complexity
[82]. WireGuard provides easy configuration where connections are established by ex-
changing simple public keys[78].
Security Features: WireGuard employs a fixed, non-negotiable suite of modern cryp-
tographic primitives. ChaCha20 for symmetric encryption, typically using a 256-bit key
(AEAD with Poly1305 for authentication). Curve25519 for Elliptic Curve Diffie-Hellman
(ECDH) key exchange. BLAKE2s for hashing and keyed hashing functions [78]. While
WireGuard’s cryptographic suite is modern and efficient, it does not meet CNSA Suite
requirements mandated by the CSfC MSC Capability Package.
Performance Analysis: WireGuard is widely recognized for its high performance, of-
ten outperforms both OpenVPN and IPsec implementations[78]. In comparative tests by
NordVPN, WireGuard delivers significantly higher throughput than OpenVPN [79]. Wire-
Guard has lower CPU load and less impact on system resources. That’s why it is suitable
for a wide range of devices.
Other VPN Protocols
In addition to the widely used VPN protocols, several legacy protocols are available. PPTP
and L2TP are the most notable examples. These protocols have been used historically
for secure communications. However, due to significant limitations in security and perfor-
mance, these protocols are generally not recommended for modern or classified networks.
PPTP: Point-to-Point Tunnelling Protocol (PPTP) is one of the earliest VPN protocols
developed by Microsoft, widely known for its simplicity and quick setup. PPTP typically
uses Microsoft Point-to-Point Encryption (MPPE) for confidentiality and MS-CHAPv2 for
authentication[83]. However, both MPPE and MS-CHAPv2 have well-documented vul-
nerabilities. Due to security weaknesses and vulnerabilities, PPTP should not be used
for transmitting sensitive or classified information [84].
L2TP: Layer 2 Tunnelling Protocol (L2TP) is a tunnelling protocol and it does not provide
any encryption or confidentiality when used alone [85]. It is an extension of the Point-to-
Point Protocol (PPP) and was designed to combine features of PPTP and Cisco’s Layer 2
Forwarding Protocol (L2F). L2TP is almost always implemented in conjunction with IPsec.
This configuration is known as L2TP/IPsec. While L2TP/IPsec offers improved security,
its dual-layer architecture introduces complexity. Administrators must manage two sepa-
rate protocol stacks (L2TP for tunnelling and IPsec for encryption), which can complicate
configuration and troubleshooting [86]. Given these drawbacks, modern deployments
typically use IPsec directly, without the additional overhead of L2TP.
4.2.3
Network Monitoring Tools
A key security requirement of the MSC Capability Package is the integration of effective
network monitoring and incident response, as outlined in Section 3.3. The CSfC Con-
tinuous Monitoring Annex [14] highlights the need of establishing a baseline for normal
40
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:51==
network behavior, detecting misconfigurations, and identifying unauthorized or abnormal
activity in classified environments. To meet these requirements, a secure and reliable
monitoring solution must be included in the MSC architecture.
This section reviews widely used open-source tools for monitoring, SIEM, and intrusion
detection. We evaluate each tool based on its core features, security functions, and com-
patibility with CSfC-aligned systems. Rather than an exhaustive survey, this is a focused
review of tools that are most relevant to the MSC Capability Package and the scope of
this thesis. A summary of the evaluated tools is provided in Table 4.4.

Snort
Snort is a widely used open-source Intrusion Detection and Prevention System (IDS/IPS)
known for its signature-based detection capabilities [87]. It inspects network traffic in
real time and alerts or blocks based on known attack signatures. It can provide reliable
real-time alerts for suspicious traffic patterns and is often integrated into a firewall (e.g.,
OPNSense has a built-in Snort package). Snort can inspect packet headers and un-
encrypted protocol fields, but cannot decrypt payloads. That’s why snort can effectively
detect and capture ESP packets through appropriate rules or signature definitions in MSC
CP solutions.
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
41
==PAGE:52==
While Snort offers reliable detection of known threats, it provides limited metadata. For
full visibility and incident analysis, it is often paired with SIEM tools like Elastic Stack or
Security Onion [87]. Snort supports CSfC continuous monitoring by detecting network-
based attacks and suspicious activity.
Suricata
Suricata is a high-performance open-source tool for IDS/IPS and Network Security Mon-
itoring (NSM) [88]. Suricata performs real-time threat detection through deep packet in-
spection and protocol analysis [89].
Suricata is designed with a multi-threaded architecture, allowing it to efficiently process
large volumes of network traffic. As a result, it is often preferred over Snort in scenar-
ios where performance and scalability are critical. However, Suricata generally requires
more powerful hardware resources, whereas Snort remains a more lightweight option for
smaller or moderately loaded networks [87]. Suricata generates detailed logs in JSON for-
mat, including flow data, DNS queries, and HTTP requests, which are valuable for SIEM
integration [90]. However, Suricata does not have a native GUI and is often integrated
with other tools similar to the Snort.
Zeek
Zeek is a powerful, open-source network analysis framework that serves as a Network
Security Monitoring (NSM). Zeek uses dynamic protocol detection, traffic scripting, and
behavioural analysis to provide deep monitoring into network activity [91]. Zeek is a pas-
sive monitoring system and it acts as a network sensor [92]. It generates detailed logs
essential for meeting CSfC continuous monitoring requirements.
Zeek is highly extensible via its scripting language, allowing analysts to write custom logic
for detecting anomalies. Due to the complexity of its scripting and analytical capabilities,
Zeek requires technical knowledge and has a steep learning curve.
Wazuh
Wazuh is an open-source security platform that combines XDR and SIEM capabilities. It
is primarily focused on endpoint security and host-based intrusion detection [93]. It uses
a scalable agent-based architecture, where lightweight agents collect data and report to
a central server [94].
Wazuh supports log analysis, vulnerability detection, Security Configuration Assessment
(SCA), malware detection, and incident response. While it can collect some network-
related logs from endpoints (e.g., netstat output, host firewall logs), it does not inspect
traffic directly and is not a replacement for network-based sensors like Suricata or Zeek.
Elastic Stack (ELK Stack)
The Elastic Stack (ELK Stack) is a widely used open-source platform for real-time log
management, analytics, and visualization [95]. It includes different components to satisfy
this capabilities. Beats or Elastic Agent collects data from various sources. Logstash
then parses, and transforms this data. Elasticsearch indexes and stores the processed
data, so we can search and analyze data. Finally, Kibana provides the user interface for
querying the data, creating visualizations, and building interactive dashboards [95].
The Elastic Stack provides high scalability and resiliency, and it is capable of handling
massive volumes of data across distributed clusters [96]. While the Elastic Stack pro-
vides strong capabilities in its open-source tier, some advanced features such as auto-
mated threat detection, machine learning, and alerting are only available through Elastic’s
commercial licenses [97]. Elasticsearch is also resource-intensive, requiring significant
CPU, memory, and storage [96].
42
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:53==
Security Onion
Security Onion is a free and open-source Linux distribution designed for threat hunting,
enterprise security monitoring, and log management [98]. It is not a single tool but rather
an integrated platform that bundles a suite of powerful open-source security tools.
It can be deployed as a standalone solution or in a distributed architecture, offering flexi-
bility and scalability for different network environments. Its distributed setup (Figure 4.1)
allows multiple sensor and management nodes to collaborate in collecting, processing,
and storing security data efficiently.
Figure 4.1: Security Onion Distributed Architecture
Security Onion delivers comprehensive network and endpoint visibility by integrating signature-
based NIDS (Suricata), rich protocol metadata (Zeek or Suricata’s NSM capabilities), full
packet capture (Stenographer), and automated file analysis (Strelka). It also offers host
visibility via the Elastic Agent [98]. Security Onion is well-suited to address many CSfC
monitoring requirements due to its integrated nature.
Although Security Onion has high capabilities, it is resource-intensive. Running multiple
components simultaneously requires significant hardware capacity. In a distributed archi-
tecture, the ManagerSearch node requires at least 8 CPUs, 16 GB of RAM, and 200 GB
of storage. Similarly, the Sensor node requires a minimum of 4 CPUs, 12 GB of RAM,
and 200 GB of storage.

4.3
Summary and Key Contributions
In Chapter 4, we conducted a literature review to explore the landscape of tools and
technologies relevant to implementing a secure multi-site connectivity solution.
We started the review with an analysis of CSfC-approved commercial solutions (Section
4.1). Key vendors include Cisco, Juniper Networks, Palo Alto Networks, and HPE Aruba,
all offering CSfC-approved IPsec VPN gateways and firewalls. We also explored EU-
based alternatives from vendors. While these products are not on the CSfC list, they hold
significant European security certifications.
Then, we reviewed open-source tools (Section 4.2), which present potential alternatives
for building MSC solutions. For firewall and VPN gateway solutions (Section 4.2.1), we
analyzed platforms like pfSense, OPNsense, VyOS, IPFire, and OpenWrt, comparing
their features, base OS, security update frequency, ease of use, and hardware require-
ments (Table 4.2). Regarding VPN protocols (Section 4.2.2), we focused on IPsec (via
StrongSwan), OpenVPN, and WireGuard, evaluating their security features, performance
characteristics, and alignment with CSfC cryptographic requirements (Table 4.3). Finally,
we reviewed network monitoring tools (Section 4.2.3). We evaluated their core functions,
detection methods, and fit for CSfC continuous monitoring needs.
RQ4: What are the characteristics, advantages, and limitations of commercial so-
lutions for secure multi-site connectivity?
We provided an overview of CSfC-approved commercial products (Section 4.1). We iden-
tified characteristics and advantages like formal certifications (Common Criteria, FIPS val-
idation) and dedicated vendor support. We also discussed the limitations, such as high
licensing and hardware costs, and the potential for vendor lock-in. We also explored EU-
based alternatives (Table 4.1). While these products are not CSfC-approved components,
they possess relevant European security certifications.
RQ5: What are the existing open-source solutions for secure multi-site connectiv-
ity, and how do open-source solutions perform in terms of performance and secu-
rity?
We conducted a review of open-source solutions in Section 4.2.
In this section, we
discussed firewall/VPN gateway solutions based on features, security, and community
support (Table 4.2). We also analyzed key open-source VPN protocols, including IPsec
(StrongSwan), OpenVPN, and WireGuard, and we detailed their security mechanisms
and typical performance profiles (Table 4.3). We finally reviewed open-source tools for
network monitoring and discussed how they can help meet the CSfC continuous monitor-
ing requirements (Table 4.4).
While this chapter provides a general overview of the performance and security features of
open-source tools, the existing literature lacks comprehensive comparisons under con-
trolled environments. Although a few studies have benchmarked OpenVPN and Wire-
Guard, such evaluations are still limited. In Chapter 8, we address this gap by conducting
experiments to compare firewall and VPN solutions in a controlled setting, allowing for
a more accurate assessment of their performance and security. We also note that per-
formance can vary depending on the underlying hardware and that secure configuration
often requires advanced technical expertise.

==PAGE:55==
5
Methodology
In this chapter, we outline the research strategy and methodology we use throughout the
rest of the thesis. After reviewing the problem and the MSC CP framework, we focus
on implementing and evaluating commercial and open-source solutions in a dual-layer
secure network. Our main goal is not just to deploy the solutions, but also to evaluate
and compare them based on CSfC requirements and real-world feasibility. Therefore, it is
important to follow a clear and structured research method. This chapter describes how
we carry out the research, evaluate each solution, and compare the results in a consistent
and structured way.
5.1
Research Methodology
Figure 5.1: Research Methodology Diagram
In this thesis, we follow clear steps of methodology while implementing and analysing the
Multi-Site Connectivity solution using Commercial and Open-source tools. We illustrated
the overall process in Figure 5.1.
One of the main goals of this thesis is to deliver a hands-on implementation and com-
parative analysis of secure multi-site connectivity solutions. Before proceeding with im-
plementation and evaluation, we first establish a dedicated set of metrics and evaluation
criteria. These metrics guide all subsequent testing and analysis. These metrics ensure
that our testing addresses both the security and performance requirements relevant to
CSfC. The next section presents these criteria in detail and explains how they align with
the thesis objectives.
After defining metrics, we proceed to the design and implementation of both commercial
and open-source solutions. We simulate the commercial solution using Cisco Modeling
Labs with a fully virtualized infrastructure. For the open-source solution, we set up a
virtual network on VMware ESXi using pfSense and other open-source tools. Each system
is tested under controlled scenarios to gather data on performance and behaviour. In
addition, we analyze both solutions in terms of their security posture and compliance with
the CSfC MSC Capability Package requirements.
Once the implementations are completed and evaluated, we compare the two solutions
based on performance, security, cost, and other relevant considerations. This comparison
highlights the trade-offs between open-source and commercial tools, helping us under-
stand how each performs in different aspects. By the end of the section, we gain a clear
view of which solution is better suited for specific needs, budgets, and goals.
In the final phase of the thesis, we extend the open-source solution with centralized mon-
itoring and additional security testing. Since the initial setup does not address all CSfC
requirements, this extended phase allows us to validate whether the open-source archi-
tecture can meet those standards.
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
45
==PAGE:56==
5.2
Metrics and Evaluation Criteria
In the following sections, we analyze various open-source tools and compare them with
a commercial implementation of the CSfC Multi-Site Connectivity solution. A clear set of
evaluation metrics is needed to ensure a fair and consistent comparison. These metrics
provide a basis for assessing performance, security, and compliance with CSfC require-
ments. Among various potential measures, we selected metrics based on the require-
ments defined by the Danish Defence Intelligence Service (DDIS), as outlined in Section
2.2. This section introduces the evaluation criteria and the tools used to measure them
across both the open-source and commercial solutions.
5.2.1
Performance and Operational Metrics
Network performance can be affected by several factors, such as the complexity of en-
cryption algorithms, hardware capabilities, and data packet sizes. To compare open-
source and commercial implementations effectively, we first needed to define a consis-
tent and relevant set of performance and operational metrics. We selected these metrics
based on CSfC requirements, discussions with DDIS, and practical limitations within the
virtual lab environment. In this section, we present these metrics and explain how they
were applied to evaluate both solutions.
Table 5.1: Summary of Performance and Operational Metrics

Throughput (Bandwidth)
Throughput represents the maximum rate at which data is successfully transmitted over
the network, typically measured in megabits per second (Mbps) [99]. In secure envi-
ronments, especially those employing dual-layer IPsec, encryption introduces processing
overhead that may reduce overall throughput. To evaluate this, we used iperf3 in both
TCP and UDP modes, measuring data transfer rates to assess the impact of encryption
on network performance.
Latency / Round-Trip Time (RTT)
Latency represents the time it takes for a data packet to travel from the source to the desti-
nation. Round-trip time (RTT) is the two-way delay [99]. Latency is influenced by propaga-
tion delay and processing delay from encryption/decryption at each layer [100]. Dual-layer
IPsec often increases latency due to the extra encryption processing and packet handling
in two devices. We measured latency using ICMP echo requests (ping) and analyzed
packet capture timestamps to identify differences between encrypted and unencrypted
setups.
46
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:57==
Jitter
Jitter refers to the variation in packet arrival times. It is particularly important for real-time
applications. Encryption processing can introduce variability if CPU load fluctuates. We
assessed jitter using iperf3 in UDP mode [101]. This tool helps simulate continuous data
flows and detect fluctuations in packet arrival times across the tunnel. High jitter could
indicate inconsistent processing times in the virtual environment.
Packet Loss
Packet loss indicates the percentage of packets that fail to reach their destination [102].
However, under high encryption load, especially with dual-layer setups, packet drops may
occur due to limited processing capacity or buffer overflow. We assessed packet loss
using both iperf3 (UDP mode) and ping.
CPU Utilization
Encryption and decryption are CPU-intensive processes. High CPU utilization may indi-
cate that encryption tasks are consuming excessive processing power, potentially lead-
ing to performance bottlenecks. We monitored CPU usage on the GUI provided by both
Open-source and Commercial tools. The process is explained in detail in the performance
analysis sections of each implementation.
Encryption Overhead
Encryption overhead refers to the additional time and resources required to secure data
compared to unencrypted transmission. It is often measured as the difference in through-
put between encrypted and unencrypted scenarios. We evaluated encryption overhead
by comparing the throughput tests from iperf3 under encrypted and unencrypted scenar-
ios to inspect encryption load. This analysis helped us understand the trade-offs between
security and performance in our network implementations.

The Multi-Site Connectivity Capability Package (MSC CP) and its supporting annexes
define a comprehensive set of security requirements that any compliant solution must
fulfill. The MSC CP is designed to support key security features (Section 3.3), including
confidentiality, integrity, and auditability. However, availability is not included by design in
this package.
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
47
==PAGE:58==
A security assessment is performed to check how well each solution meets these security
features. Table 5.2 summarizes these security features and metrics. These metrics are
identified based on the security requirements of MSC CP. To evaluate how effectively
each solution satisfies these core security principles, a structured security assessment
is necessary. We derived the assessment metrics from the MSC CP. An overview of the
corresponding security features and their evaluation metrics is provided in Table 5.2.
Confidentiality
Maintaining the confidentiality of classified data as it traverses untrusted networks is a
fundamental requirement of the MSC CP. This is primarily achieved through the use of
dual-layer encryption and strict access control policies.
• Strength and Configuration of Encryption: The solutions need to verify that the
cryptographic algorithms comply with the CNSA Suite (MSC-VG-1). This includes
validating support for strong encryption standards such as AES-256 or ChaCha20.
Both open-source and commercial implementations are assessed to ensure correct
usage and configuration of these algorithms.
• Access Control Mechanisms: MSC solution discusses the need for strict controls
on accessing system components and management interfaces (MSC-DM-3, MSC-
DM-17, and MSC-SR-18). The assessment evaluates whether the implemented
solutions enforce role-based access, prevent unauthorized administrative access,
and maintain detailed logs of access attempts.
• Secure Data Transmission: All data exchanged between sites must be fully en-
crypted using NSA-approved algorithms, as outlined in Table 3.1. The assessment
verifies that encryption is consistently applied across both encryption layers and that
no unencrypted traffic is transmitted over untrusted networks.
Integrity
Maintaining data integrity prevents unauthorized modification. The MSC CP requires data
in transit to be protected against tampering through cryptographic integrity mechanisms.
This is achieved by using NSA-approved cryptographic algorithms from the CNSA Suite
as listed in Table 3.1. Both the Inner and Outer IPsec layers are expected to provide this
functionality (MSC-VG-11, MSC-VG-12). During evaluation, each solution is assessed on
its ability to maintain integrity across all communication layers.
Auditability and Logging Granularity
Auditability refers to the ability to record and monitor system and network logs.
Ac-
cording to the MSC CP and the Continuous Monitoring Annex, logging must be imple-
mented across all critical components as specified by CM-MP requirements and CM-
LN-17. These logs should be sent to centralized monitoring systems (i.e., Gray SIEM
or Red SIEM) for correlation and analysis. The assessment focuses on how complete,
detailed, and structured these logs are, ensuring they provide sufficient information to
support threat detection and forensic investigations.
Additional Security Considerations
In addition to the core features of confidentiality, integrity, and auditability, several other
security metrics contribute to the overall security posture of a CSfC-compliant solution.
• Maintenance and Development Status: we evaluate whether the selected tools
and platforms are actively maintained and regularly updated. As required by MSC-
GD-22, solutions must support timely patching, especially for vulnerabilities in cryp-
tographic libraries and operating systems. Regular maintenance is critical to ensur-
ing long-term security in production environments.
48
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:59==
• Compliance with Security Policies and Guidelines: We assessed each solu-
tion for alignment with CSfC MSC requirements, as well as its annexes, and other
relevant security compliances. This includes checks for physical separation of en-
cryption layers (MSC-PS-11), the use of different vendors for each encryption layer
(MSC-PS-6), and diversity in operating systems and cryptographic libraries (MSC-
PS-7 and MSC-PS-8) to mitigate the risk of shared vulnerabilities. While not all
requirements could be tested due to scope limitations, the selected metrics repre-
sent the most essential considerations based on the needs and requirements of
DDIS.
