6
System Architecture and Setup
In this chapter, we present the system architecture of the secure multi-site connectivity so-
lution. MSC CP supports various configurations for multi-site connectivity solutions based
on operational needs, including centralized management and independent site models.
Additionally, the use of an Outer Firewall is required when the Black Network connects to
the internet (MSC-SR-12), and a Gray Firewall is necessary when different security levels
exist within the Red Network (MSC-PF-23). The architecture developed in this thesis is
based on the requirements defined by the Danish Defence Intelligence Service (DDIS),
the MSC CP, and its supporting annexes. As a result of discussions with DDIS, the design
follows a centralized management configuration (see Section 3.2.1). This approach aligns
with the principles described in both the MSC CP and the Enterprise Gray Implementation
Annex [11].
6.1
Network Topology
The general network topology is designed based on the CSfC MSC Capability Package.
The goal is to provide a secure, scalable, and centrally managed solution that protects
classified data using dual-layer encryption. We applied this topology consistently across
both the open-source and commercial implementations developed in this thesis. As illus-
trated in Figure 6.1, the design consists of three logically separated network domains.
Our network topology consists of separate Red, Gray, and Black networks. The Red
Networks (192.168.4.0/24 and 192.168.5.0/24) handle unencrypted classified data, while
the Gray Networks (192.168.2.0/24 and 192.168.3.0/24) carry data encrypted once. The
Black Network (192.168.1.0/24) is an untrusted layer used for transporting twice-encrypted
data via dual IPsec tunnels. Since the Black Network is not connected to the internet and
each site has only one Red Network, neither a Gray Firewall nor an Outer Firewall is re-
quired in this architecture. This thesis uses two sites for the multi-site connectivity setup,
but the design is scalable to support additional remote sites.
Figure 6.1: Network Architecture

==PAGE:62==
The IP addressing scheme used for this network is detailed in Table 6.1. We restricted ad-
dress ranges and ports according to requirements (MSC-PF-1, MSC-PF-2 in Table 3.2).
This network setup demonstrates a centralized management model, which is a key re-
quirement of the Enterprise Gray Implementation Annex. However, we introduced the
full centralized monitoring and management configuration later in Chapter 10. In the ini-
tial implementation, the sites were configured as independently managed. That’s why
Gray2-MW and Red2-MW were provisioned for local management and used only during
the initial configuration phase.

As shown in Figure 6.1 and Table 6.1, the management networks are physically and logi-
cally separated from the data networks. This separation aligns with the MSC requirements
MSC-DM-3, MSC-DM-4, and MSC-DM-5, which mandate the isolation of management
and data planes.
Each encryption component is accessed exclusively via its corresponding management
network, in compliance with MSC-SR-18. The Red Management Workstation (Red MW)
is responsible for managing Red1-IEC and Red2-IEC, while the Gray Management Work-
station (Gray MW) manages Gray1-OEC and Gray2-OEC, in alignment with MSC-DM-17.
In addition, the architecture ensures that no unused or unassigned network interfaces are
present, fulfilling MSC-PF-2.

Hardware and Software Resources
We completed the implementation of this thesis entirely within a virtualized network en-
vironment to simulate both open-source and commercial secure connectivity architec-
tures. As detailed in the limitations section (Section 2.3.2), no physical routers or CSfC-
certified hardware components were used. We used the following hardware and software
resources throughout the implementations.
• Lenovo ThinkSystem SR630 V3: The system runs on a Lenovo ThinkSystem
server, which provides the necessary compute resources to host multiple virtual
machines.

– Specifications: 32-core Intel(R) Xeon(R) Gold 5416S CPU, 1 TB RAM.
• VMware ESXi 8.0 Hypervisor: A bare-metal hypervisor installed directly on the
Lenovo ThinkSystem server. It allows for the creation and management of multiple
virtual machines on a single physical server.
• VMware Workstation Pro 17: Used to configure and manage the virtual machines
deployed on the ESXi hypervisor. It also supports custom networking between com-

and firewalls, are emulated within the CML environment.
• Ubuntu 24.04.1: End devices are using this Linux-based operating system.

configurations.
• OPNSense 25.1: An alternative open-source firewall and router platform used in
baseline experimentation to evaluate performance and security characteristics.
6.3
Virtual Environment Setup
In this thesis, we used the virtual environment, which is hosted on a Lenovo ThinkSystem
server running the VMware ESXi hypervisor. We deployed all components of both the
commercial and open-source solutions as virtual machines (VMs) within this environment.
The initial server configuration and installation of VMware ESXi were performed by the
DTU Compute IT Department. Because another team handled this part, we do not include
detailed steps related to hardware provisioning and ESXi installation in this thesis.
After the base configurations were ready, we began creating the virtual network and de-
ploying virtual machines. Both implementations were deployed on the same server. The
main difference between them is how the network and devices are simulated. Figure 6.2
visualizes the virtual environment created within ESXi.
We implemented the commercial solution entirely within Cisco Modeling Labs (CML),
which runs as a single high-performance Linux-based virtual machine. As CML inter-
nally emulates all network devices, only one VM and a single virtual network are needed
for this setup.
In contrast, the open-source solution uses VMware ESXi’s built-in networking features. It
involves multiple VMs and separate virtual networks to represent the distinct architectural
layers defined in the MSC CP.
Despite these differences, both implementations start with the same process of creating
virtual networks and virtual machines in VMware ESXi. Configuration procedures for the
virtual environment are detailed in Appendix B, which outlines the shared setup steps.
Specific implementation and design decisions are presented in Chapter 7 for the com-
mercial solution and Chapter 8 for the open-source solution.

7
Commercial Solutions
In this chapter, we examine how commercial solutions can be used to implement a se-
cure multi-site connectivity architecture based on the NSA’s CSfC Multi-Site Connectivity
(MSC) Capability Package.
The NSA maintains a list of CSfC-approved commercial components [15], and the MSC
Capability Package requires using components from this CSfC Components List (MSC-
PS-1, MSC-PS-3). However, due to the high cost and complexity of acquiring real com-
mercial hardware, we used Cisco Modeling Labs (CML) for simulation in this thesis. This
decision was also recommended by the Danish Defence Intelligence Service (DDIS).
This chapter explains the implementation of the MSC CP within the CML simulation en-
vironment. This is followed by a comprehensive evaluation of the system’s performance,
security effectiveness, and cost. These evaluations are conducted based on the defined
metrics outlined in Section 5.2. Although we conducted the analysis within a simulated
environment, the results provide valuable insights into the feasibility and characteristics
of the CSfC MSC CP for deployment in real-world classified environments.
7.1
Implementation of Commercial Tools
In this section, we present the implementation of the CSfC MSC solution using the Cisco
Modeling Labs (CML) (version 2.7 Personal+) simulation environment. Our network topol-
ogy (Figure 7.1) and implementation follow the architectural principles and security re-
quirements defined in the CSfC MSC Capability Package, as detailed in Chapter 3. Full
configuration details are provided in Appendix C to avoid disrupting the main thesis flow.
The following subsections highlight key implementation aspects, showing how we met the
relevant MSC CP requirements. Our goal is to build a functional dual-layer IPsec VPN
architecture aligned with the core principles of the CSfC MSC CP.
Figure 7.1: CML Network Topology
7.1.1
Establishing the CSfC MSC Network Topology in CML
Our first step is to design and implement the network infrastructure within CML, adhering
to the CSfC MSC CP’s layered security model.
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
55
==PAGE:66==
As explained in Chapters 3.2 and 3.4, this model requires separate Red, Gray, and Black
networks. Our CML topology (Figure 7.1) implements these layers as follows:
As explained in Chapters 3.2 and 3.4, this model requires separate Red, Gray, and Black
networks. Our CML topology (Figure 7.1) implements these layers as follows:
• Red Networks (e.g., 192.168.4.0/24 and 192.168.5.0/24): Trusted internal net-
works carrying unencrypted classified data and connected endpoints.
• Gray Networks (e.g., 192.168.2.0/24 and 192.168.3.0/24): Networks where data is
encrypted once by the Inner Encryption Components (IECs).
• Black Network (192.168.1.0/24): Represents the untrusted transport layer where
twice-encrypted data is transmitted.
The specific IP addressing scheme for all components was already shown in Table 6.1.
CML offers a limited set of virtual appliances, and our computational resources were also
constrained. Within these limits, we selected the most capable routers available: Cisco
CSR1000V for the Outer Encryption Components (OECs) and IOSv for the Inner En-
cryption Components (IECs). This allowed us to implement operating system diversity
(IOS XE for CSR1000V and classic IOS for IOSv). This aligns with MSC-PS-7, which re-
quires different operating systems to minimize shared vulnerabilities. Although MSC-PS-6
recommends using different vendors, we selected these routers based on the available
images in Cisco Modeling Labs (CML).
We allocated 4 GB of RAM and 4 vCPUs to each encryption device, according to the
resource constraints of CML. After placing the nodes, we configured IP addresses and
routing (Appendix C.2.2). On the OECs, we set static routes to the remote site’s Gray
network and IECs (MSC-OR-8). For the IECs, we used OSPF to manage dynamic routing
within and between Red and Gray networks, enabling scalability as recommended in the
Enterprise Gray Annex (EG-AR-22) [11].
We configured secure management access for all encryption devices. As described in
Appendix C.2.3, we configured SSHv2 on all routers, generated 2048-bit RSA keys, and
restricted VTY line access to permit only SSH. We established dedicated management
interfaces (Gray1-OEC to 192.168.20.0/24 and Red1-IEC to 192.168.40.0/24). These
configurations meet MSC-SR-18 by using dedicated management interfaces and comply
with MSC-DM-3 by ensuring management access originates from the correct internal net-
works. To strengthen authentication and eliminate default credentials, we applied strong
passwords generated using the scrypt algorithm. This satisfies MSC-GD-21 by ensuring
password strength and complies with MSC-SR-5 by removing all default credentials.
7.1.2
Key Generation and Distribution
Effective IPsec tunnel security depends on proper cryptographic key management. The
CSfC Symmetric Key Management Requirements Annex [13] recommends using Pre-
Shared Keys (PSKs), particularly encouraging their use for at least one encryption tunnel
to enhance quantum resistance (PSK-U-7). In our CML setup, we used PSKs to authen-
ticate IKEv2 peers for both the inner and outer tunnels.
As detailed in Appendix C, we generated strong 256-bit PSKs using openssl rand -base64
32. In real-world deployments (Figures 3.5 and 3.6), keys are generated by a secure
Key Generation Solution (KGS) and distributed to Device Managers (DMs). However,
in the CML simulation, we directly incorporated generated PSKs into the IKEv2 keyring
configurations of our Cisco routers, as manual distribution is allowed when encryption of
56
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:67==
PSKs is not feasible (PSK-GD-14). For example, on Gray1-OEC, we configured a crypto
IKEv2 keyring and manually added the PSK for its peer, Gray2-OEC. This ensured mutual
authentication for IKEv2 exchanges. While we simplified key distribution, the generation
of strong, random keys aligns with the principles of secure key management.
7.1.3
IPsec Tunnel Configuration
To secure data transmission in line with MSC CP requirements, we implemented a dual-
layer IPsec VPN tunnel setup. Each tunnel consists of two phases: Phase 1 for IKEv2
negotiation and Phase 2 for IPsec data encapsulation. The full configuration steps are
documented in Appendix C.2.5.
IKEv2 (Phase 1) configuration handles the negotiation of security parameters and au-
thenticated key exchange. Following the Symmetric Key Management Annex [13], we
used RFC 8784-compliant IKEv2 for quantum-resistant key exchange (MSC-VG-9). We
applied CNSA Suite algorithms (aes-gcm-256 for encryption, prf sha512, and DH group
24), aligning with MSC-VG-1 and MSC-VG-18. We also limited IKE SA lifetimes to 24
hours (MSC-VG-13).
The IPsec (Phase 2) configuration defines how data is protected. We defined the tunnels
using esp-aes 256 for confidentiality and esp-sha-hmac for integrity. Due to CML simu-
lation node limitations, we were unable to use esp-aes-gcm-256, which is recommended
by MSC-VG-18. We used ESP in Tunnel Mode, as required by MSC-OR-1 and MSC-IR-
1. Additionally, we set the IPsec SA lifetime to 1 hour to align with MSC-VG-14, which
requires a maximum lifetime of 8 hours. We mirrored these configurations on both ends
of the tunnels to ensure the reliable establishment and operation of the dual IPsec layers.
7.1.4
Firewall and Other Configurations
In the MSC CP architecture, firewalls are critical for enforcing network segmentation and
traffic control. As we discussed in system architecture (Chapter 6), we did not use dedi-
cated firewall appliances separate from the VPN gateways. In CML implementation, we
utilized the built-in Access Control List (ACL) capabilities of Cisco IOSv and CSR1000v
routers to implement necessary firewall functions. We detail our firewall configurations
in Appendix C.2.6. Although not applied in this simulation, real-world deployments may
require dedicated firewalls. For example, an Outer Firewall is needed if the Black Net-
work connects to the public internet (MSC-SR-12), and a Gray Firewall is required when
managing multiple Red networks with different classification levels (MSC-SR-7).
• Network Separation and Isolation: We configured stateful firewall rules to strictly
separate the Red, Gray, and Black networks and block unauthorized access. For ex-
ample, on Gray1-OEC, we blocked direct IP traffic between its Black (192.168.1.0/24)
and Gray (192.168.2.0/29) networks. This ensures only encrypted traffic crosses
network layers, meeting MSC-OR-3, MSC-OR-6, and MSC-SR-17.
• Restricted Management Access: We limited administrative access to encryption
devices to specific management networks and workstations (MSC-SR-18, MSC-
DM-7, and MSC-PF-8). For example, Gray1-OEC accepts SSH (TCP port 22) con-
nections only from the Gray Management Network (192.168.20.0/24). Unauthorized
attempts are blocked and logged.
• Control of Other Services and Protocols: We disabled DNS lookups to prevent
unwanted external queries, aligning with MSC-SR-19, MSC-SR-20.
• Packet Integrity during Transit: To avoid IP fragmentation, we configured the
IPsec DF bit for all IPsec traffic on both IECs and OECs (MSC-SR-23).
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
57
==PAGE:68==
With these configurations, we completed the implementation of the MSC-compliant secure
multi-site connectivity solution. Tunnel validation and security testing are discussed in
Section 7.2.1.
7.2
Analysis of Commercial Solution
The previous section described our implementation of the CSfC Multi-Site Connectivity
(MSC) architecture using commercial tools in the CML environment. In this section, we
evaluate the solution in terms of security, performance, and cost. The goal is to assess
its effectiveness in simulation, its compliance with CSfC MSC requirements, and the es-
timated costs for real-world deployment.
7.2.1
Security Assessment and Compliance with CSfC MSC Requirements
We assess the security posture of the commercial setup based on the metrics defined
in Section 5.2.2. Since the implementation in Section 7.1 already covers compliance
with MSC CP requirements, this section focuses on validating the setup and performing
feasible tests within CML.
CML lacks internet access or external tool integration, so advanced testing (e.g., intru-
sion detection, external scanning, or anomaly simulation) was not possible. Furthermore,
physical security and NIAP-certified hardware cannot be evaluated in this simulation en-
vironment. Instead, we provide a high-level discussion based on standard commercial
practices and product documentation from CSfC-approved vendors.
Confidentiality
Confidentiality ensures that classified information is protected from unauthorized disclo-
sure of data-in-transit or data-at-rest[10].
• Strength and Configuration of Encryption:
We implemented CNSA Suite-compliant encryption using IKEv2 with AES-GCM-
256, SHA512 PRF, and DH Group 24; and IPsec ESP with AES-256 and SHA-
HMAC. Tunnel status was verified using show crypto ikev2 sa and show crypto
ipsec sa, confirming successful negotiation of parameters (Figures 7.2 and 7.3).
Figure 7.2: Validation of IKEv2 Security Associations and IPsec Tunnel Status
58
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:69==
Figure 7.3: Command Line Output: Validation of IPsec SAs
Packet captures confirmed that only encrypted ESP packets passed through the
Gray and Black networks. No plaintext data was observed (Figure 7.4).
Figure 7.4: Network Traffic Capture: Demonstrating Encrypted ESP Packets Traversing
the Gray Network Through VPN Tunnels
• Access Control Mechanisms: We implemented a strict firewall rules to limit access
to the OECs and IECs via dedicated management networks, meeting MSC-SR-18,
MSC-DM-3, and MSC-DM-17. SSHv2 access was permitted only from authorized
management workstations.
To validate this, we attempted SSH access from an authorized workstation (192.168.20.2)
and an unauthorized endpoint (192.168.4.4). The authorized connection succeeded
and the unauthorized one was blocked (Figure 7.5) and logged (Figure 7.6).
• Secure Data Transmission: To test end-to-end encryption in action, we used scp
to transfer a test file (file1.txt) from Site1-Red to Site2-Red (Figure 7.7). Packet
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
59
==PAGE:70==
Figure 7.5: Denied SSH access attempt to Red1-IEC from unauthorized Site1-Red
Figure 7.6: Log messages on Red1-IEC showing denied SSH attempts due to ACL 103.
captures taken during the transfer showed only ESP packets traversing the Gray
and Black networks (captures are similar to Figure 7.4). The file was received un-
modified (see Appendix C.2.6 and Figure C.9), confirming successful and secure
transmission through the dual-encrypted tunnels.
Figure 7.7: Secure Data Transmissiong using SCP to Site2
Integrity
Integrity ensures that data remains unaltered during transmission. In the MSC architec-
ture, IPsec enforces this through cryptographic protections. In our setup, we configured
IKEv2 with prf sha512 and AES-GCM for integrity of negotiation messages. We used
esp-sha-hmac to ensure per-packet integrity for IPsec-encrypted traffic.
We verified these settings using show crypto ikev2 sa and show crypto ipsec sa com-
mands (Figures 7.2 and 7.3). Additionally, to validate integrity at the application level, we
transferred a file between Red endpoints using scp (Figure 7.7). The transfer completed
without errors, confirming that data integrity was maintained. While more advanced in-
tegrity testing is not feasible in CML, the successful IPsec SA establishment with HMAC
satisfies core MSC CP requirements.
Auditability
Auditability refers to the ability of the system to log and monitor security-relevant events.
According to the Continuous Monitoring Annex (CM-MP Requirements, CM-LN-17), all
components must generate logs to support incident detection.
Due to CML’s limitations, we did not implement a full SIEM solution. However, we verified
that key logging functions were active. For instance, an unauthorized SSH attempt to
Red1-IEC was correctly blocked and logged (Figure 7.6).
60
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:71==
Other Security Features
• Maintenance and Development Status: The requirement MSC-GD-22 stresses
that the implementing organization must test and apply security patches to all com-
ponents. While not directly applicable to a static CML environment, Cisco software
offers vendor support and a defined patching lifecycle, which is essential when se-
lecting CSfC-approved components.
• Compliance with Security Policies and Guidelines: While we detailed many
CSfC requirements throughout the implementation and assessment, several addi-
tional controls were also satisfied:
– Network Segmentation and Isolation: We strictly enforced the separation of
Red, Gray, and Black networks using ACLs to block unauthorized routing or
data flow between zones, in line with MSC-OR-3, MSC-OR-6, and MSC-SR-7.
– Service Hardening: To minimize the attack surface, we disabled DNS lookups
on all routers, complying with MSC-SR-19 and MSC-SR-20.
– Credential Protection: We set strong passwords and hashed them using the
‘scrypt‘ algorithm, supporting MSC-SR-5 and Also we set password of at least
112 random bits (MSC-GD-21).
Limitations of Security Assessment in CML
While our CML-based simulation provided a solid foundation for validating configuration
and functional security, some important security aspects remain out of scope:
• Product Authenticity and NIAP Certification: We used virtual Cisco images rather
than certified physical devices listed in the CSfC Components List (MSC-PS-1 and
MSC-PS-16).
• Physical Security Requirements: Requirements such as hardware protection (MSC-
GD-1), controlled physical access (MSC-GD-2), and physical separation of IEC and
OEC (MSC-PS-11) were not assessable in this virtual environment.
• Advanced Adversarial Testing: Our focus was on configuration accuracy and
functional validation. We did not perform penetration tests or simulate advanced
attacks such as DoS, cryptographic exploits, or IKEv2 tampering.
7.2.2
Performance Evaluation
As defined earlier in Section 5.2.1, we use several metrics to evaluate the performance
and operational capabilities of both commercial and open-source solutions. In this section,
the commercial implementation within Cisco Modeling Labs (CML) is tested and analyzed
based on these metrics.
We conducted performance tests to evaluate the simulated commercial solutions built
using IOSv and CSR1000v (both 4 GB RAM, 4 vCPU). All tests were executed from the
Site2-Red endpoint (192.168.5.4) to the Site1-Red endpoint (192.168.4.4).
1. Round-Trip Time (RTT) Test: To measure baseline latency, we conducted a basic
ICMP ping test between the two endpoints. Figure 7.8 shows the RTT results.
2. TCP Throughput Test: Using the iperf tool, we measured TCP throughput be-
tween the same endpoints. As shown in Figure 7.9, the average throughput was
844 Kbits/sec, with 1.75 MB transferred over 17.4 seconds. This result is signifi-
cantly lower than expected from real commercial hardware and highlights the CPU
and memory constraints within the CML environment.

and zero packet loss, indicating a stable and consistent connection, even under

Performance Limitations
While the CML provides a practical and cost-effective platform for testing, it does not
represent the full performance capabilities of real commercial hardware. Commercial VPN
appliances can reach throughput rates of 100 Gbps or more (Table 7.2), depending on
their configuration and specifications. In contrast, our CML simulations achieved only
10–20 Mbps (Table 7.1). This gap is due to several limiting factors:
• Product Authenticity: The CAT8000v and other routers used in our tests are
software-based images, not physical appliances. Their performance in CML does
not reflect real-world capabilities.
• Environment Constraints: CML limits CPU and memory resources, which restricts
the ability to simulate high-throughput or large-scale scenarios.
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
63
==PAGE:74==
• vCPU Limitations: Simulated routers rely on shared virtual CPUs, whereas actual
devices use dedicated hardware. This significantly enhances processing power and
throughput.
While we acknowledge these limitations, the results still provide valuable insights into
relative performance and resource requirements.
7.2.3
Cost Analysis
Cost is an important factor when evaluating multi-site connectivity solutions, as it includes
not only the initial investment but also deployment and ongoing operational expenses.
However, as defined in the scope of this thesis (Section 2.3.1), licensing, deployment,
and maintenance costs are considered out of scope.
For implementation and testing, we used Cisco Modeling Labs (CML) Personal Plus,
which provided a cost-effective way to simulate the commercial CSfC architecture. The
annual subscription for CML Personal Plus was $349 USD [103]. This solution was hosted
on a Lenovo ThinkSystem SR630 V3, which is a general-purpose enterprise server rather
than a specialized network encryption or routing appliance designed for high assurance
environments. Consequently, a direct cost analysis of our lab environment is not compa-
rable to the dedicated components in real-world CSfC solutions.
Given these considerations, we need to focus on the potential costs of acquiring CSfC-
compliant commercial components rather than the simulation setup. We present a cost
breakdown in Table 7.2, covering both high-end and budget-friendly commercial devices.
We also included EU-based alternatives. These examples are based on products from
the CSfC Approved Components List [15] and relevant European vendors identified in our
literature review (Section 4.1).
It’s important to emphasize that this table is not exhaustive and represents publicly avail-
able list prices. Some EU-based alternatives do not have listed prices, so we estimated
their costs using similar products and marked these with an asterisk (*). Actual prices may
vary depending on the chosen configuration, support packages, and vendor negotiations.
The purpose of this table is to give a rough idea of the expected investment.
In line with CSfC requirements, component selection must meet the criteria of MSC-PS-6
and MSC-PS-7, which require that the components originate from different manufacturers
and operate on different platforms or operating systems. Additionally, MSC-PS-10 spec-
ifies that the Gray Firewall must also come from a different manufacturer than the Inner
Encryption Component. As a result, identical or closely related products cannot be used
for multiple encryption layers.
When evaluating commercial options, there is a strong correlation between cost and
performance. Organizations must balance their budget with operational needs such as
throughput, latency, connection capacity, and security assurance.
The high-end CSfC-approved setup exceeds $885,000, includes high-performance hard-
ware such as the Forcepoint NGFW 3410, Aruba 7280 Mobility Controller, and high-
capacity Juniper SRX5400 devices. This configuration supports massive throughput (ap-
proximately 450 Gbps firewall and 120 Gbps IPsec), making it suitable for enterprise-level
or national-scale deployments. The Juniper SRX5800 [125] offers even higher capacity,
with 3.36 Tbps firewall throughput and 699 Mbps IPsec throughput. However, we ex-
cluded it from the comparison table due to unavailable pricing information.
On the other end, a more affordable CSfC-compliant setup can be built for around $43,000.
This configuration uses entry-level devices like the Aruba 7005 and Juniper SRX1500.

While these components deliver significantly lower performance, they remain suitable for
small to medium-sized organizations with moderate traffic demands.
European alternatives do not offer the same level of robustness as premium CSfC com-
mercial products, but they offer a practical balance between performance and cost. A
high-performance EU-based solution can cost approximately $207,600 and still deliver
strong performance (up to 100 Gbps firewall and 40 Gbps IPsec). These solutions in-
clude products from vendors like Stormshield, Clavister, and Lancom. They are certified
by national agencies such as ANSSI (France) [36] and BSI (Germany) [126]. Although
these products are not CSfC-listed, they meet equivalent security requirements under EU
frameworks. This makes them a strong choice for EU organizations or anyone who needs
to follow local EU regulations.
In summary, top-tier CSfC solutions offer the best security guarantees and performance at
a high cost. Low-cost CSfC options are more accessible but come with clear performance
limitations. EU-based alternatives offer a balanced trade-off between performance and
cost. They present a viable option for secure deployments in European contexts, partic-
ularly where adherence to EU certification frameworks is sufficient and CSfC approval is
not mandatory.
7.3
Summary and Key Contributions
In this chapter, we focused on implementing and analyzing a CSfC Multi-Site Connectivity
(MSC) solution using commercial tools. We used Cisco components in the Cisco Model-
ing Labs (CML) environment to simulate a realistic setup and test the MSC architecture
without requiring physical CSfC-approved hardware (Section 7.1).
Following the implementation, we conducted an analysis of the commercial solution (Sec-
tion 7.2). This included security validation and compliance assessment within the CML
(Section 7.2.1). We performed performance evaluations (Section 7.2.2), while noting that
CML’s resource constraints significantly impacted these results compared to real hard-
ware capabilities. Finally, we presented a cost analysis (Section 7.2.3) by providing an
estimated cost breakdown for real-world CSfC-approved and EU-based commercial hard-
ware components.
In this chapter, we made key contributions to the thesis by addressing the following re-
search questions:
RQ1: How can a secure multi-site connectivity solution be designed and imple-
mented to meet CSfC MSC requirements using both commercial and open source
tools?
We addressed designing and implementing an MSC-compliant solution using commercial
tools (Section 7.1). We demonstrated how MSC requirements can be applied in practice
within the CML simulation environment. We explained steps for configuring dual IPsec
tunnels, routing, keying, and firewall rules according to CSfC guidelines.
RQ4: What are the characteristics, advantages, and limitations of commercial so-
lutions for secure multi-site connectivity?
We answered Research Question 4 throughout the whole chapter.
Section 7.1 describes the end-to-end implementation of a CSfC MSC-compliant architec-
ture using Cisco IOSv (IEC) and CSR1000v (OEC) devices. It outlines the configuration
of dual IPsec tunnels, routing, keying mechanisms, firewall policies, and secure SSH-
66
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:77==
based management access. This setup reflects the architecture described in Chapter 3,
demonstrating how commercial tools can be used to meet CSfC requirements in practice.
In section 7.2, we highlight key advantages in terms of security and performance of com-
mercial tools. These solutions provide strong security through certified cryptographic al-
gorithms (e.g., AES-GCM, SHA-512). Additionally, they are capable of delivering excep-
tionally high throughput, making them suitable for large-scale deployments.
However, we also identified several limitations. As discussed in Section 7.2.3, commercial
solutions often come with significant costs and risks of vendor lock-in. Due to budget
constraints, we could not deploy physical appliances and instead used Cisco Modeling
Labs (CML) to simulate the setup.
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
67
==PAGE:78==
68
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:79==
8
Open-Source Solutions
In this chapter, we explore how open-source tools can be used to implement the CSfC
Multi-Site Connectivity (MSC) architecture.
The open-source tools are excluded from the CSfC Components list due to the lack of
Common Criteria or NIAP certifications. However, CSfC guidelines state that open-source
tools may be listed if they undergo the necessary evaluations [15]. With this context, we
examine how open-source components can be selected, implemented, and validated to
meet MSC CP requirements and build a secure multi-site solution.
We follow a structured approach throughout this chapter. In Section 8.1, we present how
we selected open-source firewall/router platforms and VPN protocols, based on a combi-
nation of literature review and our experiments. After selecting the tools, we implement
a dual-layer MSC-compliant architecture using only open-source components in Section
8.2. Finally, in Section 8.3, we evaluate our implemented open-source solution in terms
of performance, security features, and costs.
8.1
Selection of Open-Source Tools
In this section, we review and select suitable open-source firewall solutions and VPN pro-
tocols for use in our MSC CP architecture. The goal is to understand how well different
tools meet the performance and security requirements defined in our methodology (Sec-
tion 5.2). Our selection is based on findings from the literature review (Section 4.2) and
our practical experimentations. While many tools are discussed in prior work, most stud-
ies do not provide direct performance comparisons or testing under same conditions. To
address this, we conduct our own experiments in a simplified and controlled environment.
We use the results from both our literature review and our experimental testing to guide our
selection. This approach allows us to choose the most suitable open-source components,
which we use to implement and evaluate a fully functional MSC solution aligned with CSfC
requirements.
8.1.1
Open-source Firewall and VPN Gateway Solutions
In the CSfC MSC architecture, encryption components may also provide robust firewall
capabilities if a separate firewall device does not exist [10, page 16]. In our System Archi-
tecture (Figure 6.1), we are not using separate firewall devices. Therefore, we focused
on selecting open-source tools capable of supporting both functionalities.
In the literature review (Section 4.2.1), we reviewed several open-source firewall solutions
with VPN Gateway capabilities. We excluded solutions that are no longer maintained (e.g.
IPCOP, Smoothwall) or are not fully free (e.g., Untangle, Sophos). Our analysis focused
on OPNsense, pfSense, VyOS, IPFire, and OpenWrt. We have a detailed discussion in
the literature review, and key insights from these can be summarized as:
• pfSense and OPNsense are both robust, FreeBSD-based firewall and routing platforms.
pfSense is known for its powerful core features, extensive VPN support (including IPsec with
NSA Suite B cryptography), and stable performance. OPNsense offers similar functionality
with a more modern user interface and integrated tools like Suricata. Studies suggest that
OPNsense is well-suited for smaller organizations or those seeking easier management. In
contrast, pfSense is preferred for complex enterprise environments due to its performance
and customization flexibility [59, 60].
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
69
==PAGE:80==
• VyOS: A Debian-based platform designed for enterprise-grade routing (BGP, OSPF) and
firewalling. Although VyOS is open source, access to pre-built LTS images typically re-
quires a paid license. Some commercial appliances (e.g., Cloudberry MIL CR-6228 [55])
integrate VyOS and claim NIAP and CSfC certifications. However, due to its CLI-focused
management and licensing model, VyOS is less suitable for the scope of this thesis.
• IPFire: A lightweight Linux-based firewall that uses color-coded zones to simplify network
management. It performs well in high-throughput scenarios but lacks built-in monitoring
tools and extensive package support. This makes it less suited for classified environments.
• OpenWrt is a highly extensible Linux distribution for embedded devices, offering core rout-
ing and firewall capabilities. However, it lacks integrated monitoring and a user-friendly
interface without additional packages. It is more suitable for low-power or special-purpose
deployments.
Based on our review, pfSense emerged as the best choice due to its strong performance,
support for CSfC-compliant cryptography, and broad community adoption. We selected
pfSense for our implementation. However, since OPNsense offers similar features, we
experimented with both tools using defined performance metrics (see Section 8.1.3). If
both tools perform similarly, pfSense remains the preferred option due to its proven relia-
bility and wider use in comparable scenarios.
8.1.2
VPN Protocols
As we reviewed in Section 4.2.2, IPsec, WireGuard, and OpenVPN are the most widely
used VPN protocols in secure networking. Legacy protocols like PPTP and L2TP were
excluded due to known security flaws or lack of native encryption. We provided a com-
parative overview of IPsec, WireGuard, and OpenVPN in Table 4.3.
• IPsec - StrongSwan: IPsec is a mature and comprehensive protocol suite that operates
at the network layer. StrongSwan is a widely used open-source implementation. It sup-
ports CNSA Suite algorithms and FIPS validation, making it ideal for regulated environ-
ments. IPsec is explicitly approved for CSfC MSC CP encrypted tunnels. While IPsec
offers high performance, especially with hardware acceleration (e.g., AES-GCM), it can be
CPU-intensive and complex to configure.
• OpenVPN utilizes SSL/TLS and the OpenSSL library for flexible, secure connections with
various cryptographic options, including AES and X.509 certificates, aligning with some
CSfC preferences. However, its performance is generally lower than IPsec or WireGuard.
It is not explicitly approved for CSfC solutions and requires careful configuration to meet
cryptographic standards.
• WireGuard is a modern VPN protocol designed for simplicity, high performance, and strong
security using state-of-the-art cryptography (ChaCha20, Poly1305, Curve25519). Its mini-
malist codebase aims to reduce the attack surface. It often beats IPsec and OpenVPN in
speed and uses less CPU. However, it does not support CNSA algorithms, which limits its
applicability under CSfC guidelines.
For the CSfC MSC CP framework, IPsec is the most suitable VPN protocol due to its
explicit approval and support for the required CNSA cryptographic suite. While WireGuard
offers superior performance and simplicity, and OpenVPN provides excellent flexibility,
their limitations regarding CSfC cryptographic mandates or direct approval make them
less optimal for primary encrypted tunnels in this context. In the next section, we validate
this choice through controlled performance testing.
8.1.3
Experimentation of Selected Tools
In this section, we transition from theoretical selection to practical validation. We aim to
determine whether pfSense, when configured with IPsec, can deliver acceptable perfor-
mance while meeting the security and architectural requirements outlined in Section 2.2.
70
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:81==
To evaluate this, we conducted a series of tests in a simplified virtual network deployed
on VMware ESXi. The test setup (Figure 8.1) reflects a minimal implementation of the
MSC architecture. We measured throughput and performance for pfSense with IPsec,
and then repeated the same tests using OpenVPN and WireGuard on the same platform.
This allowed for a fair comparison of all protocols under identical conditions.
We also included OPNsense in the experiments due to its architectural similarity to pf-
Sense. While pfSense remains our primary choice for its features, we tested OPNsense
to assess whether it offers better performance or usability with the same VPN settings.
These experiments help us validate whether pfSense and IPsec can maintain the balance
between strong security and realistic performance expected in a CSfC-compliant system.
Figure 8.1: Experiments Network Topology

We conducted all experiments on a VMware ESXi 8.0 hypervisor running on a Lenovo
ThinkSystem SR630 V3. The detailed hardware specifications are already provided in
Section 6.2. Using the ESXi environment, we deployed a virtual network illustrated in
Figure 8.1. In this network, the following Virtual Machines are used:
• pfSense Firewall/Gateway VMs (version 2.7.1)
• OPNSense Firewall/Gateway VMs (version 25.1)
• Client machines running Ubuntu 24.04.1
We designed eight distinct test configurations (Table 8.1) to evaluate performance under
varying conditions.

The deployment and initial configuration steps of virtual machines are similar to Appendix

Figure 8.2: VMWare ESXi pfSense server (Gray1-OEC) configuration
Testing Metrics and Procedure
We evaluated the selected setups using the performance and operational metrics defined
earlier in Section 5.2.1. Our goal was to compare the performance of different VPN proto-
cols and open-source tools under controlled test conditions. The key performance metrics
we focused on for these experiments are:
• Latency/Roud-Trip Time: We measured RTT using ping from the Gray1-VM to the Gray2-
VM. The average latency was recorded both during unencrypted traffic and under active
VPN tunnel usage to evaluate encryption-related latency.
• Throughput: We used iperf with TCP traffic to determine maximum achievable bandwidth
through the VPN tunnel, accounting for encryption overhead.
– Server: iperf -s on Gray2-VM
– Client: iperf -c 192.168.3.2 on Gray1-VM
• Jitter: We tested jitter using iperf in UDP mode to assess packet timing variability through
the encrypted tunnel.
– Server: iperf -s -u on Gray2-VM
– Client: iperf -c 192.168.3.2 -u on Gray1-VM
• CPU Utilization: We observed real-time CPU usage on pfSense and OPNsense dash-
boards during each throughput test. This metric helps assess the processing load of each
VPN protocol under different system resource profiles.
We conducted each test multiple times and reported the average values to ensure con-
sistency and precision. While the virtual environment provides consistent test conditions,
it does not fully replicate hardware-specific behaviors or performance under real-world
traffic diversity. Therefore, results should be interpreted as indicative comparisons rather
than absolute benchmarks. In the next sections, we present the experimental configura-
tion, setup for each scenario.

both the firewall and the VPN protocol.
8.2
Implementation of Open-Source Tools
In this section, we describe the implementation of the Multi-Site Connectivity (MSC) ar-
chitecture using open-source components deployed on a virtualized ESXi environment.
Based on the previous evaluations, we selected pfSense as the firewall and encryption
gateway, and IPsec as the VPN protocol. The network topology for this implementation
is shown in Figure 8.13. This architecture follows the high-level system design described
in Chapter 6 and meets the requirements defined in Chapter 3.
Figure 8.13: Network Architecture Open Source Environment
In this setup, we focus only on the secure data network. Centralized management is
not included here and is discussed separately in Chapter 10. This architecture contains
temporary Gray2-MW and Red2-MW to create the initial configurations.
8.2.1
Establishing the CSfC MSC Network Topology on ESXi
The virtual environment for this implementation was hosted on a Lenovo ThinkSystem
SR630 V3 server, which runs VMware ESXi version 8.0. The hardware and software
specifications of this host environment are identical to those described in Section 6.2.
We first created the necessary virtual network infrastructure using ESXi’s built-in network-
ing features. We then deployed each component as a separate virtual machine and con-
figured them according to the logical design shown in Figure 8.13.
Virtual Network Infrastructure
To achieve the necessary network segmentation based on the MSC CP, we created dis-
tinct virtual switches (vSwitches) and port groups within VMware ESXi for each network
78
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:89==
layer, as shown in Figures 8.14 and 8.15. This ensures logical separation of the Black,
Gray, Red, and Management networks and aligns with the logical separation defined in
Chapter 6.
Figure 8.14: Virtual switches con-
figuration in ESXi for segmented
network layers
Figure 8.15: Port group and Net-
work Assignment
• Black Network: We used vSwitch-Black and the Black Network port group for sim-
ulating the untrusted external layer (192.168.1.0/24).
• Gray Networks: We created vSwitch-Gray1 with Gray Network 1 (192.168.2.0/24)
for Site 1, and vSwitch-Gray2 with Gray Network 2 (192.168.3.0/24) for Site 2.
These networks carry once-encrypted data and located between Red and Black
Networks.
• Red Networks: We created vSwitch-Red1 with port group Red Network 1 (192.168.4.0/24)
for Site 1, and vSwitch-Red2 with port group Red Network 2 (192.168.5.0/24) for Site
2. These correspond to classified internal networks where data is unencrypted form.
• Management Networks: We created dedicated vSwitches and port groups for each
distinct management network segment (e.g., Gray Network 1 Mgmt for 192.168.20.0/24,
Red Network 1 Mgmt for 192.168.40.0/24, etc.), as per the IP addressing scheme in
Table 6.1. We ensured the management networks are separate from the data net-
works. This separation aligns with the MSC requirements MSC-DM-3, MSC-DM-4,
and MSC-DM-5, which mandate the isolation of management and data planes.
To serve as encryption components, we deployed four pfSense virtual machines, each
with 4 GB RAM and 4 vCPUs:
• Outer Encryption Components (OECs): Gray1-OEC and Gray2-OEC handle the
first encryption layer between Gray and Black networks.
• Inner Encryption Components (IECs): Red1-IEC and Red2-IEC handle the sec-
ond encryption layer between Red and Gray networks.
Figure 8.16 shows the VM configuration for Gray1-OEC. In this setup, we connected three
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
79
==PAGE:90==
virtual network adapters to the encryption component: Adapter 1 (WAN) links to the Black
Network, Adapter 2 (LAN) connects to the Gray Network, and Adapter 3 is assigned to
the Management Network.
Figure 8.16: Gray1-OEC VM deployment on ESXi
We acknowledge that using pfSense for both Outer and Inner Encryption Components
does not fully comply with MSC-PS-6 (requiring different vendors) and MSC-PS-7 (re-
quiring different operating systems). However, we deliberately chose a uniform pfSense-
based approach to keep this implementation simple for our thesis and entirely open source.
In real-world deployments, we should follow these MSC Requirements.
We also deployed Ubuntu 24.04 LTS virtual machines to function as endpoint devices
in the Red Network (Mete-Red1-VM, Mete-Red2-VM) and as management workstations
(Gray1-WM, Gray2-WM, Red1-WM, Red2-MW). Each of these VMs was provisioned with
4 GB RAM and 4 vCPUs.
Figure 8.17 presents an overview of all VMs we used to build the secure multi-site test
environment. The blue arrows highlight the VMs currently used in this implementation.
Components related to centralized management and monitoring are introduced later in
Chapter 10.
Network Configuration
We configured IP addresses according to Table 6.1 from Chapter 6 and illustrated in Figure
8.13. We assigned static IP addresses to each pfSense VM on the LAN and Management
interfaces to maintain consistent routing and firewall rules.
Static IP addresses were configured for the LAN and Management interfaces of the pf-
Sense VMs. For ease of configuration in the virtual lab environment, we allowed the
WAN interfaces to obtain IP addresses via DHCP. The same approach was applied to
the Ubuntu-based endpoint and management VMs, which also used DHCP for address
assignment.
MSC-PF-1 mandates the use of restricted address ranges. While we followed this prin-
ciple in the overall addressing scheme, we used larger /24 subnets for our Gray/Red

==PAGE:91==

Networks to allow flexibility while testing multiple VMs. In a real-world deployment, these
address ranges should be strictly limited to only the required addresses. We also ensured
that any unused interfaces on pfSense VMs were disabled to align with MSC-PF-2. Figure
8.18 shows the interface assignments in the Gray1-OEC pfSense console.

8.2.2
IPsec Tunnel Configuration
After the network architecture is established, we need to create nested dual-layer IPsec
tunnels according to the MSC CP Requirements. First, we configure the outer IPsec
tunnel between the OECs (Gray1-OEC and Gray2-OEC), then we create an inner IPsec
tunnel between the IECs (Red1-IEC and Red2-IEC).
Outer IPsec Tunnel Configuration (OEC-to-OEC)
To configure the outer IPsec tunnel, we accessed the Gray1-OEC and Gray2-OEC de-
vices through their respective management workstations (Gray1-MW and Gray2-MW).
We first applied the configuration steps on Gray1-OEC, then mirrored the setup on Gray2-
OEC. This tunnel connects the Gray1 network 192.168.2.0/24 with the Gray2 network
192.168.3.0/24.
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
81
==PAGE:92==
The configuration follows a standard two-phase IPsec process.
Phase 1 (IKE SA Proposal) Configuration: In Phase 1, we defined the IKE Security
Association (IKE SA) settings, which govern how peers authenticate and establish secure
communication. This includes selecting encryption algorithms, hashing functions, and key
exchange methods. Figure 8.19 illustrates the Phase 1 setup.
• Authentication Method: We used Pre-Shared Keys (PSKs) for IKEv2 authentication. pf-
Sense supports automatic generation of strong 256-bit keys and recommends using PSKs
that are at least 10 characters long, including a mix of uppercase letters, lowercase letters,
numbers, and special characters [127].
• Key Exchange Method: We configured AES-256, which satisfies MSC-VG-10. However,
we note that it can also be defined as AES256-GCM based on MSC-VG-18, as both are
allowed.
• Hash Algorithm: SHA384 (based on Approved Algorithms in Table 3.1 in Chapter 3).
• Diffie-Hellman (DH) Group: We selected Group 20 (ecp384), which provides strong elliptic-
curve-based key exchange.
• Lifetime: 28800 seconds (8 hours), which complies with MSC-VG-13 (maximum 24 hours).
• Split Connections: Disabled, as required by MSC-OR-7.
Figure 8.19: Overview of configured IPsec tunnels in pfSense
Phase 2 (IPsec Policy Configuration): In Phase 2, we configured the IPsec policy re-
sponsible for securing actual data traffic. This phase includes encryption and integrity
parameters for the tunnel. The configurations for Phase 2 are shown in Figures 8.19
and 8.20. We used only cryptographic algorithms from the CNSA Suite. This aligns with
the requirement MSC-VG-1, which requires the use of CNSA Suite algorithms for IPsec
encryption and authentication.
• Mode: Tunnel IPv4
• Local Network: The Gray network of the local site (e.g., 192.168.2.0/24 for Gray1-OEC).
• Remote Network: The Gray network of the remote site (e.g., 192.168.3.0/24 for Gray1-
OEC).
• Protocol: ESP (Encapsulating Security Payload), as required by MSC-OR-1.
• Encryption Algorithms: We used AES-256-GCM, which is required by MSC-VG-12. Alter-
natively, AES-256-CBC is also permitted based on MSC-VG-11.
• Perfect Forward Secrecy (PFS): Enabled with Diffie-Hellman Group 20 (ecp384).
• Lifetime: Configured as 3,600 seconds (1 hour), which meets the requirement under MSC-
VG-14 (maximum 8 hours).
For Site B (Gray2-OEC), we applied the same IPsec configuration steps. In this case,
the local network was Gray2 (192.168.3.0/24) and the remote network was Gray1
(192.168.2.0/24). We defined lifetimes on Gray2-OEC as 10% higher (e.g., Phase 1
lifetime is 31680 seconds) than on Gray1-OEC, because pfSense documentation sug-
gests this helps to avoid overlap[128].
82
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:93==
Figure 8.20: IPsec Phase 2 (Child SA) expiration and rekeying settings in pfSense
Inner IPsec Tunnel Configuration (IEC-to-IEC)
We followed a similar procedure to configure the inner IPsec tunnel between Red1-IEC
and Red2-IEC. Using Red1-MW and Red2-MW, we accessed the IEC management in-
terfaces and established a tunnel connecting the Red1 (192.168.4.0/24) and Red2
(192.168.5.0/24) networks. We ensured this tunnel follows the requirement of MSC-
IR-1, which requires ESP Tunnel mode IPsec for the Inner VPN Gateway.
We applied similar cryptographic parameters as in the outer tunnel for both Phase 1 and
Phase 2. However, to meet the MSC-PS-8 requirement, we replaced AES256-GCM with
AES256-CBC. This change is permitted under MSC-VG-11, which allows AES-CBC as
an alternative to AES-GCM. This ensures consistency and full compliance with the CNSA
Suite and the security requirements defined by the CSfC MSC Capability Package.
8.2.3
Firewall Rule Configuration
After successfully establishing the dual-layer IPsec tunnels, we configured firewall rules
on each pfSense device to meet the access control and traffic flow requirements outlined
in the CSfC MSC Capability Package. These rules ensure that only authorized and en-
crypted traffic passes between network layers. We show the firewall configuration for
Gray1-OEC. similar rules were applied to the other encryption devices in the architecture.
LAN Interface Firewall Rules
Figure 8.21 shows the LAN interface rules on Gray1-OEC. These rules define the al-
lowed traffic from the local Gray1 Network (192.168.2.0/24) to the remote Gray2 Network
(192.168.3.0/24). We created three specific rules to permit only TCP, UDP, ICMP, and
ESP packets between these networks. All other traffic is explicitly denied.
Figure 8.21: Firewall rules configured on the Gray1-OEC LAN interface
This configuration satisfies MSC-PF-11 by restricting permitted protocols. It enforces strict
segmentation between networks, ensuring that traffic from the Red network cannot tra-
verse into the Gray or Black layers without encapsulation. We ensure that only IPsec-
encapsulated packets and IKE packets are allowed to pass. These configurations satisfy
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
83
==PAGE:94==
requirements MSC-PF-13, MSC-OR-2, MSC-PF-17, and MSC-PF-18.
WAN Interface Firewall Rules
For the WAN interface, we did not configure any rules. Only default rules are available for
this interface, as shown in Figure 8.22. By default, pfSense blocks all traffic on the WAN
interface except for essential services. It automatically includes hidden rules that allow
IPsec-related traffic, such as IKE over UDP port 500 and ESP (protocol 50), to ensure the
tunnel functions correctly.
Figure 8.22: Firewall rules configured on the Gray1-OEC WAN interface
This configuration fulfills MSC-PF-3 by filtering traffic on the Black Network interface. It
prevents packets from bypassing decryption when arriving from the Black Network (MSC-
OR-5) and ensures that outbound traffic is encrypted before leaving the Gray Network
(MSC-OR-2). Additionally, it blocks unwanted multicast traffic (MSC-PF-9) and guaran-
tees that the OEC only establishes connections with its designated peer (MSC-OR-10).
IPsec Interface Firewall Rules
pfSense creates a logical IPsec interface when a tunnel is established. We defined rules
on this interface to control which traffic is allowed through the tunnel. Figure 8.23 shows
the rules for the IPsec interface on Gray1-OEC. These rules allow only TCP, UDP, ICMP,
and ESP traffic originating from the remote Gray2 Network and destined for Gray1. This
ensures the tunnel is used only for explicitly defined and secure traffic, further enforcing
MSC-PF-11.
Figure 8.23: Firewall rules configured on the Gray1-OEC IPsec interface
We also added rules to permit management traffic from Gray2 Management (192.168.30.0/24)
to Gray1 Management (192.168.20.0/24). These rules support centralized logging and
monitoring, which we explain in more detail in Chapter 10.
84
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:95==
Management Interface Firewall Rules
To secure administrative access to the Gray1-OEC, we configured specific rules on its
MGMT interface, as shown in Figure 8.23. Rules 4 and 5 ensure that only the Gray1
Management Workstation (MW) can access the Gray1-OEC configuration interface using
HTTPS. All other traffic is denied by default.
Figure 8.24: Firewall rules on the Gray1-OEC MGMT interface
These rules align with MSC-SR-18, which requires that solution components must only
be managed via dedicated management interfaces. They also satisfy MSC-DM-3 and
MSC-DM-5, which require that MWs connect from an internal interface and that the Gray
Management Network be used exclusively for managing Outer Encryption Components.
The first three rules in this figure allow communication from Gray2 Management (192.168.30.0/24)
for centralized logging. We further describe this setup in Chapter 10.

CSfC MSC architectural and cryptographic requirements.
8.3
Analysis of Open-Source Solution
In this section, we analyze the implemented open-source solution and examine the open-
source tools in the context of the CSfC Multi-Site Connectivity Capability Package (MSC
CP). We evaluate the practical viability of these solutions by assessing their performance
characteristics, security posture, and associated costs.
8.3.1
Security Assessment and Compliance with CSfC MSC Requirements
To assess the security posture of our implementation, we mapped key architectural ele-
ments to the MSC CP requirements. We focused on four critical security objectives in-
88
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:99==
troduced in Section 5.2.2: confidentiality, integrity, auditability, and compliance with MSC
CP.
Confidentiality
• Strength and Configuration of Encryption: We implemented CNSA Suite-compliant
encryption configurations for both outer and inner tunnels. In the IPsec Phase 1 set-
tings, we used IKEv2 with AES-GCM-256 encryption, SHA-512 for PRF, and Diffie-
Hellman Group 24 (ecp384). For the IPsec Phase 2 (ESP), we used AES-GCM-256
combined with HMAC-SHA for payload encryption and authentication. These con-
figurations comply with MSC-VG-1, MSC-VG-10, MSC-VG-12, and MSC-VG-18 re-
quirements. To meet the MSC-PS-8 requirement, we replaced AES256-GCM with
AES256-CBC in Inner IPsec tunnel encryption component. This change is permitted
under MSC-VG-11, which allows AES-CBC as an alternative to AES-GCM.
We validated successful tunnel establishment (Figures 8.25, 8.27) and observed
ESP packets in the Gray and Black networks (Figures 8.30, 8.31). These confirmed
that cryptographic configurations are applied and protecting data in transit.
• Access Control Mechanisms: We ensured access to each encryption device is
restricted through dedicated management networks and proper firewall rules. This
setup satisfies MSC-SR-18 (use of dedicated interfaces), MSC-DM-3 (management
from internal ports), and MSC-DM-17 (separate MWs for IEC and OEC).
For example, only Gray1-MW (192.168.20.2) was permitted to access Gray1-OEC
over HTTPS. Figure 8.33 shows the successful login log from Gray1-MW. In con-
trast, access attempts from an unauthorized VM (Red1-VM) were blocked, as shown
in Figure 8.34, confirming proper enforcement of access control policies.
Figure 8.33: Authorized access from Gray1-MW to Gray1-OEC
• Secure Data Transmission: The fundamental purpose of the MSC solution is to
ensure that all data exchanged between Red network sites is encrypted twice be-
fore traversing the untrusted Black network. We demonstrated this using an FTP
file transfer between Red1 and Red2 VMs in Section 8.2.4. The file was visible in
plaintext only within the Red network. On the Gray network, it appeared as ESP
packets, and on the Black network, it was encapsulated again with a second ESP
header. This confirms that confidentiality was maintained through layered encryp-
tion, as required by the MSC CP.
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
89
==PAGE:100==
Figure 8.34: Blocked unauthorized access from Red1-VM to Gray1-OEC
Integrity
To ensure integrity, we applied cryptographic algorithms from the CNSA Suite in both
phases of the IPsec configuration. For IKEv2 (Phase 1), we used SHA-512 as hash
function to ensure the integrity of the key exchange process.For IPsec ESP (Phase 2), the
AES-GCM provides confidentiality and data integrity for each packet. We confirmed that
the IKEv2 Security Associations (SAs) were correctly negotiated between peers, as shown
in Figures 8.25 and 8.27. Additionally, we verified the correctness of data transmission
during the FTP test, where the file was transferred without corruption or loss.
While we did not simulate active attacks such as replay or man-in-the-middle scenarios,
the use of CNSA-compliant integrity mechanisms provides strong assurance of data in-
tegrity. Future work can include these attack simulations for better validation.
Auditability
The CSfC Continuous Monitoring Annex [14] highlights the importance of comprehensive
logging and monitoring. To align with requirements such as CM-MP and CM-LN-17, our
solution ensures logging of critical events across encryption devices and management
networks. We demonstrated logging and monitoring capabilities using pfSense’s built-in
features and dedicated monitoring points.
pfSense logs network activity to support security monitoring. For example, Figure 8.33
shows a successful, authorized management login attempt, and Figure 8.34 shows an
unauthorized access attempt that was correctly denied and logged.
To enhance monitoring, we also deployed monitoring VMs with Wireshark in each network
segment (see Table 8.5) to capture and analyze network traffic. While this setup provides
essential logging and traffic inspection, we addressed full compliance with the Continuous
Monitoring Annex in Chapter 10.
Other Security Features
• Maintenance and Development Status: When selecting our open-source tools
(pfSense and Ubuntu LTS), we prioritized solutions that are actively maintained,
have strong community support, and receive regular security updates(MSC-GD-22).
This minimizes the risk of unpatched vulnerabilities and maintains long-term secu-
rity. Additionally, open-source communities often identify and resolve vulnerabilities
faster than commercial solutions due to distributed peer review and transparency.
This agility offers a practical advantage in dynamic threat environments.
• Compliance with Security Policies and Guidelines: Throughout the implemen-
tation, we aligned our configurations with the CSfC MSC Capability Package guide-
90
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:101==
lines. While many compliance aspects were discussed earlier, the following high-
lights additional key alignments not previously mentioned.
– Network Segmentation and Isolation: We enforced strict separation of the
Red, Gray, and Black networks using ESXi vSwitches and pfSense firewall
rules.
These rules blocked direct routing and unauthorized traffic between
zones, ensuring compliance with MSC-OR-3, MSC-OR-6, and MSC-SR-7.
– Disabling Unnecessary Services: We disabled non-essential services on pf-
Sense and Ubuntu machines to minimize the attack surface, following MSC-
SR-19 and MSC-SR-20.
– Password Security: We replaced default credentials and enforced strong
passwords for all administrative interfaces, meeting the requirements of MSC-
SR-5 and MSC-GD-21.
• Code Transparency and Customizability: Open-source tools like pfSense allow
full monitoring of configuration behaviour and code base. This transparency allows
for independent auditing that is often limited or unavailable in proprietary solutions.
8.3.2
Performance Evaluation
We evaluated the performance of our open-source setup using key metrics defined in
Section 5.2.1: round-trip time (RTT), throughput, jitter, and CPU utilization. All pfSense
and Ubuntu VMs were assigned 4 GB RAM and 4 vCPUs. We generated traffic between
Mete-Red1-VM and Mete-Red2-VM to conduct the tests.
Figure 8.35: Ping RTT measurement between Red endpoints
1. Round-Trip Time (RTT) Test: We used the ping command to measure latency
between Red network endpoints through the dual IPsec tunnels. As shown in Fig-
ure 8.35, we recorded an average RTT of 3.28 ms.
2. TCP Throughput Test: Using iperf, we measured the TCP data transfer rate. We
ran the iperf -s on Mete-Red2-VM. Then, from Mete-Red1-VM, we executed iperf
-c 192.168.5.4 to initiate a TCP traffic stream to the server. The average throughput
recorded was 168 Mbps (Figure 8.36).
3. UDP Bandwidth and Jitter Test: We also performed UDP-based tests to observe
jitter levels (Figure 8.36). The jitter was recorded as 0.525 ms, which is acceptable
for most applications.

When we compared the two configurations, we observed a significant reduction in through-
put and an increase in latency and jitter for the dual-layer IPsec setup. This reduction is
expected due to the overhead introduced by processing data through two IPsec tunnels.
Each encryption layer adds computation time and packet encapsulation overhead.
Despite these limitations, the results show that our open-source implementation deliv-
ers acceptable performance for virtualized environments. While real-world deployments
would require more powerful hardware to reach higher speeds (e.g., 10–100 Gbps), these
findings suggest that open-source solutions can be a viable option from a performance
perspective for secure multi-site connectivity.
8.3.3
Cost Analysis
In this section, we focus on the cost implications of the open-source CSfC Multi-Site Con-
nectivity (MSC) solution that we implemented and analyzed. Commercial solutions of-
ten involve high upfront expenses, including hardware purchases, license fees, and sub-
scriptions. In contrast, open-source tools reduce these costs by eliminating license fees,
though they may still require investment in hardware and technical expertise for setup and
ongoing maintenance.
While open-source options can be more budget-friendly initially, they can still generate
operational costs over time. However, as explained in Section 2.3.1, this thesis does not
provide a detailed breakdown of long-term deployment or operational expenses.
92
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:103==
Cost Analysis of Our Virtual Environment
We implemented and tested our solution in a virtual environment hosted on a Lenovo
ThinkSystem SR630 V3 server, which is approximately $9,638 [129]. The virtualization
platform was VMware ESXi with a vSphere 8 Standard license costing around $575 [130].
We also used VMware Workstation Pro 17.0 under a free license to manage the virtual
machines. All other components were open-source and free to use. Therefore, the overall
direct cost of our test environment remained low.
It is important to emphasize that these costs reflect an academic, experimental setup and
are not representative of a full-scale production deployment. In real-world deployments,
total costs would be much higher due to the need for enterprise-grade equipment, profes-
sional support, security certifications, and long-term operational commitments.
Cost Analysis of Real-World Open Source Implementation
In real-world deployments, open-source solutions are typically installed on physical hard-
ware rather than virtualized environments. To estimate the potential costs of a hardware-
based implementation, we identified suitable hardware options for each system compo-
nent. We followed two key criteria in selecting hardware:
• Performance: The selected hardware must offer throughput and functionality com-
parable to commercial alternatives to ensure the open-source solution remains fea-
sible and competitive. We identified hardware options for both low-performance and
high-performance setups. We selected these configurations to provide performance
levels comparable to commercial CSfC-approved solutions.
• Software compatibility: We selected hardware products that are either widely
used with or officially supported by the open-source tools used in this thesis (e.g.,
pfSense, OPNsense). While open-source solutions can run on a wide range of
hardware, we focused on devices that meet compatibility standards for stable and
reliable operation.
Table 8.7 presents the estimated cost and specifications of selected hardware compo-
nents based on publicly available product documentation and vendor listings. It should
be emphasized that this table does not represent an exhaustive review of all available
products on the market. Rather, it provides an overview of selected devices that meet the
criteria of performance compatibility and open-source software support. Other vendors
and models may offer superior specifications and could be considered based on specific
project needs, procurement requirements.
The low-performance configuration has an estimated cost of approximately $14,020. They
support up to 10 Gbps firewall throughput and around 2 Gbps of dual-layer IPsec perfor-
mance. This setup is designed for small to medium-sized networks. In contrast, the
high-performance setup reaches up to $50,000 and offers significantly higher processing
capabilities. These devices can handle up to 60 Gbps firewall throughput and achieve
4 Gbps with dual-layer IPsec tunnels. It is suitable for enterprise environments requiring
higher throughput and availability.
For the Inner Encryption Component, the low-performance configuration uses the Protectli
Vault 6P FW6D, which is priced at approximately $410 and supports up to 2.5 Gbps firewall
throughput and 1.2 Gbps for IPsec. For higher performance needs, the Protectli Vault 6P
VP6670 is a suitable alternative. It costs around $1,100 and delivers up to 20 Gbps firewall
throughput and 5 Gbps for IPsec. Both models are widely deployed with pfSense and
OPNsense due to their solid IPsec performance, hardware acceleration, and multi-port
capabilities.

The Outer Encryption Component in the low-performance setup is represented by the
Netgate 8200 MAX Security Gateway, which is priced at $1,400. This device provides up
to 18.5 Gbps firewall throughput and 3.24 Gbps for IPsec. In the high-performance setup,

of handling 26.8 Gbps firewall and 14.6 Gbps IPsec throughput. These Netgate products
are widely used for pfSense and offer stable performance for IPsec-heavy use cases.
We can use the same firewall appliances across all three zones in each configuration.
In the low-performance architecture, we selected the Deciso DEC750. This device costs
approximately $900 and delivers 10 Gbps firewall throughput with around 1.2 Gbps IPsec
performance. For the high-performance option, the Deciso DEC4280 is preferred. It is
priced at $6,000 and supports up to 60 Gbps for firewalling and 2.5 Gbps for IPsec traffic.
These systems are known for their high reliability and full compatibility with OPNsense
and other FreeBSD-based platforms.
It’s important to note that hardware costs alone do not reflect the full cost of the solu-
tion. Total Cost of Ownership (TCO) should be considered for better overview. TCO also
includes the time and expert expenses for secure setup and ongoing maintenance.
Still, our evaluation shows that open-source tools can provide secure, CSfC-aligned multi-
site connectivity at a much lower cost. Both low- and high-performance setups are fea-
sible, and offer a strong alternative to commercial solutions. A detailed comparison is
provided in Chapter 9.
8.4
Summary and Key Contributions
In Chapter 8, we explored the implementation and analysis of a CSfC MSC solution using
open-source tools. This chapter outlines our tool selection process, implementation steps,
and an in-depth analysis of the solution’s security, performance, and cost.
First, we explained our selection of open-source tools (Section 8.1). Based on the litera-
ture review in Chapter 4 and further experimentation detailed in Section 8.1.3, we chose
pfSense as the firewall and VPN gateway platform. For the VPN protocol, we selected
IPsec, primarily for its alignment with CSfC’s cryptographic requirements.
We then implemented the solution in a virtualized environment using VMware ESXi (Sec-
tion 8.2). This setup included the full CSfC MSC network architecture, consisting of Red,
94
Analysis, Design and Implementation of a Secure Multi-Site Connectivity Solution
==PAGE:105==
Gray, and Black networks. We validated the setup by confirming the correct operation of
IPsec tunnels and the use of dual encryption (Section 8.2.4).
Finally, we analyzed the open-source solution (Section 8.3). This assessment included
a security assessment confirming its adherence to core CSfC principles (Section 8.3.1),
performance evaluation (Section 8.3.2) and discussion of potential costs for a real-world
deployment (Section 8.3.3).
In the following chapter, we conduct a detailed comparison between open-source and
commercial tools.
RQ1: How can a secure multi-site connectivity solution be designed and imple-
mented to meet CSfC MSC requirements using both commercial and open source
tools?
This chapter addressed the design and implementation of an MSC-compliant solution us-
ing open-source tools(Section 8.2). We explained detailed steps for configuring network
setup, encryption component configuration, secure IPsec tunnels establishment, and fire-
wall rule deployment according to CSfC guidelines.
RQ5: What are the existing open-source solutions for secure multi-site connectiv-
ity, and how do open-source solutions perform in terms of performance and secu-
rity?
In Chapter 4.2, we already reviewed different open-source firewall and VPN tools. Then,
in this chapter (Section 8.1), we tested these tools and shared performance results in
Table 8.4. Based on these tests, we selected the most suitable tools for our setup.
We also performed a security assessment of the implemented open-source solution (Sec-
tion 8.3.1) to confirm that the setup meets CSfC requirements by using CNSA-compliant
IPsec encryption.
Finally, we evaluated the performance of our dual-layer open-source IPsec solution in
a virtualized environment (Section 8.3.2), offering insights into throughput, latency, and
jitter. The results show that open-source tools can be a practical and cost-effective option
for secure multi-site connectivity.

9
Comparison of Commercial and Open
Source Solutions
In this chapter, we compare the commercial and open-source implementations of the
CSfC Multi-Site Connectivity (MSC) solution. This chapter directly answers Research
Question 6 (Section 2.1).
We organize the comparison into two parts. First, we present a side-by-side evaluation
of our implementations, where we built and tested both solutions in a virtualized environ-
ment. Second, we assess real-world deployment considerations, including cost, perfor-
mance, and security, based on literature and publicly available specifications of hardware
products. In this analysis, our goal is to provide practical insights that organizations can
use to choose between commercial and open-source options, depending on their security
requirements, operational preferences, and available resources.
9.1
Evaluation of Virtual CSfC MSC Implementations
In our virtual lab environment, we implemented and analyzed both commercial and open-
source versions of a CSfC-compliant multi-site connectivity solution. The commercial
solution was simulated using Cisco Modeling Labs (CML) with virtualized Cisco routers
(CSR1000V and IOSv). The open-source alternative was deployed using pfSense virtual
machines hosted on VMware ESXi.
Security
Both implementations followed the CSfC MSC Capability Package using CNSA Suite-
compliant cryptographic settings (AES-256-GCM, SHA-384, and Diffie-Hellman Group
20). This ensures the core security principles of confidentiality, integrity, and authenti-
cation are satisfied. Table 9.1 maps some selected MSC CP requirements with security
principles.

as pfSense and OPNsense.
